Was Musa Doubtful or Certain? Razi's Response
Razi presents a dilemma to refute the objection about Musa's request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that Musa's request for vision implies doubt or impropriety, and why this defense upholds prophetic integrity.

Core Idea
Prophetic requests must be interpreted with perfect knowledge, not doubt or rudeness.

The third objection claims that Musa's request for vision suggests either he doubted Allah's nature or acted improperly. Razi presents a dilemma showing that both possible explanations actually undermine the objection. This passage demonstrates how kalam defends prophetic impeccability by analyzing the logical implications of the request.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two horns
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: 'either...or...' The two alternatives are doubt and certainty of impossibility.
The phrase 'إما أن يقال...أو يقال' signals the dilemma.
2
Analyze the consequence of doubt
If Musa doubted, he would be ignorant of Allah—unbefitting a prophet. Reflect on why this outcome is unacceptable for prophetic status.
This shows that the objection cannot be saved by claiming doubt.
3
Analyze the consequence of certainty
If Musa was certain vision is impossible, asking for it would be improper etiquette. Consider why a rational person would not ask for the impossible.
Razi suggests a proper response would have been to ask for more proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث فجوابه أنه إما أن يقال إنه كان شاكًا في الامتناع والجواز، أو يقال : إنه كان قاطعا بالامتناع، فإن قطعنا أنه كان شاكا في الجواز لزم كونه جاهلا بالله تعالى، وهذا لا يليق بالأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - وإن قلنا : إنه كان قاطعا بامتناع الرؤية على الله تعالى كان الأدب أن يقول : رب زدني دليلًا على امتناع الرؤية فأما أن يسأل الرؤية مع العلم بامتناعها فهذا لا يليق بالعقلاء.
As for the third question, its answer is that either it is said that he was doubtful about the impossibility and permissibility, or it is said that he was certain of impossibility. If we decide that he was doubtful about permissibility, then it follows that he was ignorant of Allah Almighty, and this is not befitting the prophets (peace and blessings be upon them). And if we say that he was certain of the impossibility of seeing Allah Almighty, then proper etiquette would have been to say: 'O Lord, increase me in proof of the impossibility of vision.' As for him asking for vision while knowing its impossibility, this is not befitting rational people.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection regarding Moses' state of mind when he requested vision. Razi presents a dilemma: either Moses was doubtful about whether vision is possible or impossible, or he was certain of its impossibility. If he was doubtful, then he was ignorant of Allah's nature—unbefitting a prophet. If he was certain of its impossibility, then it would be inappropriate etiquette (أدب) to ask for what is impossible; rather, he should have asked for more proof of its impossibility. Thus, both possibilities lead to problems, so the objection is refuted. This shows that the request must be interpreted in a way that does not impugn Moses' prophethood.

Key Terms
السؤال الثالث
الشك
القطع
الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about Musa's request successfully impugns his prophethood beyond refutation.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that both possible interpretations (doubt or etiquette violation) lead to conclusions that actually support prophetic integrity, because either outcome would refute the objector's assumptions.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say would follow if Moses was doubtful about the impossibility of seeing Allah?
A) He would be considered a rational person.
B) He would be ignorant of Allah.
C) He would be deserving of punishment.
D) He would have asked for a proof.

Key Takeaway
Razi's dilemma refutes the objection by showing that both possible explanations (doubt or certainty) are inconsistent with prophetic stature, thus preserving Moses' integrity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray to Allah, do you ever ask for something while secretly knowing it may not be best? How does Musa's example—where his request is interpreted as either doubt or a breach of etiquette—challenge you to examine the sincerity of your own supplications?
?
Imagine you are in a situation where you feel you have a legitimate reason to ask something of Allah, but deep down you know it contradicts His wisdom. Would you still ask? What does this lesson teach you about the importance of aligning your requests with knowledge of Allah's attributes?
?
If you were certain that something is impossible from Allah, would you still ask for it? How does Razi's argument force you to reconsider the balance between hope and reverence in your relationship with Allah?

Refuting the Fourth Objection: Consensus on Prophetic Knowledge
Razi refutes the objection using consensus and reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the consensus (ijma) on prophetic knowledge superiority to refute the Mu'tazilite objection that Moses might not have known the impossibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
Consensus on prophetic knowledge superiority refutes the Mu'tazilite objection.

In this passage, Razi addresses the fourth objection, which likely argued that Moses lacked knowledge of the impossibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). He counters by appealing to a universal consensus of the Muslim community: prophets have more complete knowledge of Allah's essence and attributes than any layperson. Then he shows that if Moses did not know that ru'ya is impossible, ordinary Mu'tazilites would be more knowledgeable than him—a conclusion that contradicts the consensus, thus invalidating the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the claim of consensus and the logical argument that follows.
The passage is short but dense with logic.
2
Identify the consensus claim
Razi states that the umma is unanimously agreed that prophets have superior knowledge of Allah. This is the foundation of his argument.
This consensus is not about ru'ya itself but about prophetic knowledge.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Razi says: Since even ordinary Mu'tazilites know ru'ya is impossible, if Moses did not know this, they would be more knowledgeable than Moses—which contradicts the consensus. Therefore, Moses must have known.
The argument assumes that knowledge of ru'ya's impossibility is part of knowing Allah's attributes.
4
See why the objection falls
Because the conclusion of the reductio is false by consensus, the premise that Moses lacked knowledge must be false. Hence the fourth objection is invalid.
This is a classic example of argument by consensus combined with logical deduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الرابع فجوابه: أن الأمة مجمعة على أن علم الأنبياء والرسل - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - بذات الله تعالى وصفاته أتم وأكمل من علم كل واحد آحاد الأمة، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لما كان العلم بامتناع الرؤية حاصلا لكل واحد من آحاد المعتزلة فلو لم يكن حاصلا لموسى عليه السلام لكان كل واحد من آحاد المعتزلة أعرف بذات الله تعالى وبصفاته من موسى عليه السلام ولما كان هذا باطلا بالإجماع سقط هذا السؤال.
As for the fourth question, its answer is that the community (umma) is unanimously agreed that the knowledge of the prophets and messengers (peace and blessings be upon them) concerning the essence of Allah and His attributes is more complete and perfect than the knowledge of every single individual of the common people. When this is established, we say: Since knowledge of the impossibility of vision (ru'ya) is attained by every single individual of the Mu'tazila, if it were not attained by Moses (peace be upon him), then every single individual of the Mu'tazila would be more knowledgeable of the essence of Allah and His attributes than Moses (peace be upon him). And since this is false by consensus, this question falls away.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth objection, which probably argued that Moses lacked knowledge of the impossibility of seeing Allah. Razi invokes a universal consensus (إجماع) of the Muslim community: prophets have superior knowledge of Allah's essence and attributes compared to any layperson. Then he points out that even ordinary Mu'tazilites (the opponents in this debate) know that ru'ya is impossible. If Moses did not know this, then those Mu'tazilites would be more knowledgeable than Moses—a conclusion that contradicts the consensus. Therefore, the objection is invalid. This argument uses a reductio ad absurdum based on communal agreement.

Key Terms
السؤال الرابع
الإجماع
الأنبياء
المعتزلة
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the consensus is about the permissibility of ru'ya itself, not about the superiority of prophetic knowledge.
Correction: Razi's consensus is that prophets have superior knowledge of Allah's attributes. He uses that to argue Moses must have known ru'ya is impossible. The consensus is not directly about ru'ya but about comparative knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim about the knowledge of Mu'tazilites regarding ru'ya?
A) They are uncertain about its possibility.
B) They all know it is impossible.
C) They disagree among themselves.
D) They have no opinion.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the consensus that prophets know Allah best to prove that Moses must have known ru'ya is impossible, thus refuting the fourth objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that prophets know Allah better than any scholar today? If so, how does that affect the way you seek knowledge about Allah? Are you willing to accept that your own reasoning might be inferior to prophetic knowledge, especially on matters like ru'ya?
?
Razi uses consensus of the community. Have you ever rejected a consensus because you thought you knew better? What would it take for you to submit to the collective understanding of the scholars?
?
The objection implied that Moses didn't know something. Can you think of an area in your faith where you might be assuming a prophet was ignorant of a truth? How does this lesson challenge your assumptions about prophetic knowledge?

The Four Groups That Deny the Prophethood of Muhammad
Razi classifies four groups of deniers of Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of arguments used by those who deny the prophethood of Muhammad, as classified by Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
Deniers of prophethood fall into four clear types, each with a distinct rational challenge.

In this lesson, Imam al-Razi sets the stage for proving the prophethood of Muhammad by first mapping out the opposition. He identifies four distinct groups of deniers, each with a unique argument: one rejects divine obligation entirely, another says reason suffices, a third denies miracles as proof, and the fourth denies the possibility of miracles altogether. This classification helps us understand the precise nature of the objections that must be addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the four groups. Note that Razi is only listing their positions, not refuting them yet.
This is a classification passage.
2
Identify each group's core argument
After reading, list each group and summarize their argument in one sentence. This will help you see the logical progression.
3
Reflect on the strategy
Consider why Razi begins with this classification before presenting proofs. How does mapping the opposition strengthen his case?
This prepares you for the refutations to come.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الحادية والثلاثون في إثبات نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم

اعلم أن الذين ينكرون نبوة محمد طوائف:

الطائفة الأولى : الذين يقولون : المقصود من بعثة الأنبياء هو التكليف، ولكن القول بالتكليف باطل، فالقول ببعثة الأنبياء باطل.

الطائفة الثانية : الذين يقولون : التكليف جائز إلا أن العقل كاف في معرفة التكليف ؛ لأن كل ما كان حسناً فعلناه وكل ما كان قبيحاً تركناه، وكل ما لا يمكننا أن نعرف حسنه وقبحه، فإن كنا مضطرين أو محتاجين إليه اكتفينا بالقدر الدافع للضرورة والحاجة، وإن لم تكن بنا إليه حاجة، امتنعنا عنه احترازاً عن الخطر.

الطائفة الثالثة : الذين يقولون : البعثة جائزة في العقول، إلا أن الذي يمكن أن يجعل دليلاً على كون الشخص رسولاً من عند الله ليس إلا المعجزات، وهذه المعجزات لا دلالة فيها البتة على الرسالة فلا جرم بطل القول ببعثة الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - لفقدان ما يدل عليها.

الطائفة الرابعة الذين يقولون: لو أمكن حصول خوارق العادات لأمكن الاستدلال بها على صدق مدعي الرسالة، إلا أن انخراق العادات محال، فلا جرم لم يحصل ما يدل على صدقهم.
The thirty-first question: On establishing the prophethood of Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him. Know that those who deny the prophethood of Muhammad are groups: The first group: those who say: the purpose of sending prophets is obligation (taklif), but the doctrine of obligation is invalid, so the doctrine of sending prophets is invalid. The second group: those who say: obligation is permissible, but reason is sufficient for knowing obligation, because everything that is good we do, and everything that is evil we leave, and everything whose goodness and evil we cannot know, if we are compelled or in need of it we suffice with the amount that repels necessity and need, and if we have no need of it, we refrain from it as a precaution against danger. The third group: those who say: sending is permissible in the intellects, but that which can be made a proof that a person is a messenger from God is only miracles, and these miracles have absolutely no indication of the message, so necessarily the doctrine of sending prophets is invalid for lack of that which indicates it. The fourth group: those who say: if the occurrence of extraordinary phenomena were possible, then it would be possible to use them as evidence for the truthfulness of the claimant of the message, but the breaking of customary events is impossible, so necessarily nothing has occurred that indicates their truthfulness.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage introduces the thirty-first question, which aims to prove the prophethood of Muhammad. Razi first enumerates four groups of people who deny his prophethood. Each group is defined by its specific argument: (1) The first group rejects prophethood because they reject the concept of divine obligation (taklif) altogether. (2) The second group accepts the possibility of obligation but claims that reason alone is sufficient, so there is no need for prophets. (3) The third group accepts the possibility of sending prophets but denies that miracles can serve as proof of prophethood. (4) The fourth group argues that while miracles could theoretically prove prophethood, extraordinary events that break natural laws are impossible. Razi does not present his refutations here; he merely lists the positions of the opponents. This classification sets up the subsequent responses in the rest of the question.

Key Terms
نبوة محمد
التكليف
المعجزات
انخراق العادات
الطوائف المنكرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the fourth group denies miracles as proof entirely, but they actually accept that if miracles occurred, they could be evidence; they deny the possibility of miracles occurring.
Correction: The fourth group's argument is that breaking natural laws (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) is impossible, so no miracles occur; they do not deny the theoretical evidential value of miracles.

Checkpoint
According to the second group of deniers, what is sufficient for knowing obligation?
A) Prophetic revelation
B) Rational intellect alone
C) Miracles
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi identifies four distinct rational objections to prophethood that must be answered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second group claims reason is sufficient to know good and evil. In what specific area of your life have you trusted your own judgment over divine guidance, and what did that cost you?
?
The third group argues that even miracles provide no proof of prophethood. Have you ever witnessed a clear sign of God's guidance yet refused to act on it because you demanded more evidence? What would it take for you to submit fully?
?
The first group rejects the very idea that God has the right to obligate you. Is there a command of Allah that you inwardly resist because it seems ‘unreasonable’? How does your heart justify that resistance?

First Group: Denying Taklif
Razi presents the first group’s argument against prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against prophethood which denies the validity of divine obligation (taklīf).

Core Idea
If taklif is false, prophethood is false.

Imam Razi begins his enumeration of those who deny the prophethood of Muhammad by presenting the first group's reasoning. This group rejects prophethood by attacking the very concept of divine obligation (taklīf). Understanding their argument is the first step in seeing how Razi systematically refutes each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise
Notice that the first group assumes the purpose of prophets is to impose legal obligations.
2
Find the second premise
They then claim that the concept of taklīf is false.
3
See the conclusion
From these two premises, they deduce that sending prophets is false.
This is a simple syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الذين ينكرون نبوة محمد طوائف : هذه الطائفة الأولى : الذين يقولون : المقصود من بعثة الأنبياء هو التكليف، ولكن القول بالتكليف باطل، فالقول ببعثة الأنبياء باطل. الطائفة الثانية : الذين يقولون : التكليف جائز إلا أن العقل كاف في معرفة التكليف ؛ لأن كل ما كان حسنا فعلناه وكل ما كان قبيحًا تركناه، وكل ما لا يمكننا أن نعرف حسنه وقبحه، فإن كنا مضطرين أو محتاجين إليه اكتفينا بالقدر الدافع للضرورة والحاجة، وإن لم تكن بنا إليه حاجة، امتنعنا عنه احترازًا عن الخطر. الطائفة الثالثة : الذين يقولون : البعثة جائزة في العقول، إلا أن الذي يمكن أن يجعل دليلا على كون الشخص رسولاً من عند الله ليس إلا المعجزات، وهذه المعجزات لا دلالة فيها البتة على الرسالة فلا جرم بطل القول ببعثة الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - لفقدان ما يدل عليها. الطائفة الرابعة الذين يقولون: لو أمكن حصول خوارق العادات لأمكن الاستدلال بها على صدق مدعي الرسالة، إلا أن انخراق العادات محال، فلا جرم لم يحصل ما يدل على صدقهم. الطائفة الخامسة : الذين يقولون نحن ما شاهدنا شيئًا من هذه المعجزات ولا طريق لنا إلى العلم بأنها حصلت وقت دعواهم، إلا أن الناس يخبرون عن ذلك غير أن الخبر لا يفيد العلم وأقصى ما في الباب أنه يفيد الظن، إلا أن الظن غير معتبر في هذا الباب. الطائفة السادسة: جمع من الصوفية يقولون: الاشتغال بغير الله حجاب عن معرفة الله تعالى، والأنبياء يدعون الخلق إلى الطاعات والتكليف، فهم يشغلون الخلق بغير الله، ويمنعونهم عن الاشتغال بالله فوجب أن لا يكون ذلك حقا وصدقاً. الطائفة السابعة : الذين يقولون : نرى الشرائع مشتملة على أشياء لا فائدة فيها، فإن الصوم والصلاة والحج أفعال لا منفعة فيها للمعبود، وهي مضار ومتاعب في حق العباد فكان ذلك عبئًا، بل سفها، وذلك لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين، فوجب أن لا تكون هذه الشرائع من عند الله. الطائفة الثامنة : الذين يسلمون أصل النبوة إلا أنهم ينازعون في نبوة محمد
Know that those who deny the prophethood of Muhammad are sects: This first sect: those who say: the purpose of sending prophets is legal obligation (taklīf), but the claim of legal obligation is false, so the claim of sending prophets is false. The second sect: those who say: legal obligation is permissible but reason is sufficient for knowing legal obligation; for everything that is good we do it, and everything that is evil we leave it, and everything whose goodness and evil we cannot know, if we are compelled or in need of it we suffice with the amount that repels necessity and need, and if we have no need of it, we refrain from it as a precaution against danger. The third sect: those who say: sending prophets is permissible in intellects, but that which can be made a proof that a person is a messenger from God is only miracles (muʿjizāt), and these miracles have no indication at all of messengership, so necessarily the claim of sending prophets—peace and blessings of God be upon them—is invalid due to the lack of what indicates it. The fourth sect: those who say: if the occurrence of extraordinary events (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) were possible, it would be possible to use them to prove the truthfulness of a claimant of messengership, but the breaking of customs is impossible, so necessarily there did not occur what indicates their truthfulness. The fifth sect: those who say: we have not witnessed any of these miracles, nor do we have a way to know that they occurred at the time of their claims, except that people report about that, but reports do not yield knowledge, and the utmost in the matter is that they yield opinion (ẓann), but opinion is not considered valid in this matter. The sixth sect: a group of Sufis who say: occupation with other than God is a veil from knowledge of God Almighty, and the prophets call creation to acts of obedience and legal obligation, so they occupy creation with other than God and prevent them from occupation with God, so it must be that this is not true and correct. The seventh sect: those who say: we see the laws contain things in which there is no benefit, for fasting, prayer, and pilgrimage are acts that have no benefit for the worshipped, and they are harms and hardships for the worshippers, so that is a burden, rather foolishness, and that is not befitting the Most Wise of judges, so it must be that these laws are not from God. The eighth sect: those who affirm the principle of prophethood but dispute the prophethood of Muhammad.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a classification. Imam Razi lists eight groups (ṭawāʾif) that deny prophethood, each with a distinct argument. He begins with the first group, which rejects prophethood entirely on the grounds that the purpose of sending prophets is legal obligation (taklīf), but they consider taklīf false, so prophethood is false. The second group accepts taklīf but claims reason is sufficient, so prophets are unnecessary. The third group accepts the possibility of prophethood but denies that miracles prove it. The fourth group denies the possibility of miracles altogether. The fifth group asserts that we have no direct knowledge of past miracles, only reports, and reports only give opinion, which is insufficient. The sixth group, a sect of Sufis, claims that prophets distract people from God by calling them to acts of worship. The seventh group objects that religious laws contain useless hardships. The eighth group accepts prophethood in general but rejects Muhammad specifically. This enumeration sets the stage for Razi's subsequent refutations.

Key Terms
تكليف
بعثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the first group rejects prophets because they think prophets are unnecessary.
Correction: Actually, their argument is based on the falsity of taklīf itself, not just its redundancy.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the first group that denies prophethood?
A) Miracles are impossible
B) Legal obligation (taklīf) is false
C) Reason is sufficient
D) Reports only give opinion

Key Takeaway
The first group’s denial of prophethood is rooted in their denial of the validity of divine obligation (taklīf).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself resenting a divine command because it felt too heavy? Did that resentment ever push you toward questioning the whole idea of obligation? How does this group's outright denial of taklīf challenge you to examine your own attitude toward God's commands?
?
If someone today argued that prophethood is unnecessary because God does not truly command us, how would you defend the reality of taklīf from your own sense of accountability? Are there areas of your life where you already live as if God has no right to command you?
?
This group rejected prophets so they could escape obligation. Are there any truths or responsibilities you are avoiding because they demand too much? What would it cost you to fully accept God's authority over every part of your life?

Second Group: Reason Sufficient
Razi presents the second group that rejects the need for prophets by asserting reason’s sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that denies prophethood by claiming reason is sufficient for moral knowledge.

Core Idea
If reason can know good and evil, prophets are redundant.

The second group does not deny the possibility of divine obligation, but they argue that human reason is fully capable of discerning good and evil without prophetic guidance. This argument challenges the necessity of prophets for moral and legal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand their acceptance of taklif
The second group accepts that legal obligation is permissible, but they claim reason suffices.
2
See their classification of actions
They divide actions into good (known by reason), evil (known by reason), and those whose moral status is unknown.
3
Examine their practical rule
For unknown actions, they only permit what is necessary and avoid the rest to avoid danger.
This is a rationalist ethical system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الذين ينكرون نبوة محمد طوائف : هذه الطائفة الأولى : الذين يقولون : المقصود من بعثة الأنبياء هو التكليف، ولكن القول بالتكليف باطل، فالقول ببعثة الأنبياء باطل. الطائفة الثانية : الذين يقولون : التكليف جائز إلا أن العقل كاف في معرفة التكليف ؛ لأن كل ما كان حسنا فعلناه وكل ما كان قبيحًا تركناه، وكل ما لا يمكننا أن نعرف حسنه وقبحه، فإن كنا مضطرين أو محتاجين إليه اكتفينا بالقدر الدافع للضرورة والحاجة، وإن لم تكن بنا إليه حاجة، امتنعنا عنه احترازًا عن الخطر. الطائفة الثالثة : الذين يقولون : البعثة جائزة في العقول، إلا أن الذي يمكن أن يجعل دليلا على كون الشخص رسولاً من عند الله ليس إلا المعجزات، وهذه المعجزات لا دلالة فيها البتة على الرسالة فلا جرم بطل القول ببعثة الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - لفقدان ما يدل عليها. الطائفة الرابعة الذين يقولون: لو أمكن حصول خوارق العادات لأمكن الاستدلال بها على صدق مدعي الرسالة، إلا أن انخراق العادات محال، فلا جرم لم يحصل ما يدل على صدقهم. الطائفة الخامسة : الذين يقولون نحن ما شاهدنا شيئًا من هذه المعجزات ولا طريق لنا إلى العلم بأنها حصلت وقت دعواهم، إلا أن الناس يخبرون عن ذلك غير أن الخبر لا يفيد العلم وأقصى ما في الباب أنه يفيد الظن، إلا أن الظن غير معتبر في هذا الباب. الطائفة السادسة: جمع من الصوفية يقولون: الاشتغال بغير الله حجاب عن معرفة الله تعالى، والأنبياء يدعون الخلق إلى الطاعات والتكليف، فهم يشغلون الخلق بغير الله، ويمنعونهم عن الاشتغال بالله فوجب أن لا يكون ذلك حقا وصدقاً. الطائفة السابعة : الذين يقولون : نرى الشرائع مشتملة على أشياء لا فائدة فيها، فإن الصوم والصلاة والحج أفعال لا منفعة فيها للمعبود، وهي مضار ومتاعب في حق العباد فكان ذلك عبئًا، بل سفها، وذلك لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين، فوجب أن لا تكون هذه الشرائع من عند الله. الطائفة الثامنة : الذين يسلمون أصل النبوة إلا أنهم ينازعون في نبوة محمد
Know that those who deny the prophethood of Muhammad are sects: This first sect: those who say: the purpose of sending prophets is legal obligation (taklīf), but the claim of legal obligation is false, so the claim of sending prophets is false. The second sect: those who say: legal obligation is permissible but reason is sufficient for knowing legal obligation; for everything that is good we do it, and everything that is evil we leave it, and everything whose goodness and evil we cannot know, if we are compelled or in need of it we suffice with the amount that repels necessity and need, and if we have no need of it, we refrain from it as a precaution against danger. The third sect: those who say: sending prophets is permissible in intellects, but that which can be made a proof that a person is a messenger from God is only miracles (muʿjizāt), and these miracles have no indication at all of messengership, so necessarily the claim of sending prophets—peace and blessings of God be upon them—is invalid due to the lack of what indicates it. The fourth sect: those who say: if the occurrence of extraordinary events (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) were possible, it would be possible to use them to prove the truthfulness of a claimant of messengership, but the breaking of customs is impossible, so necessarily there did not occur what indicates their truthfulness. The fifth sect: those who say: we have not witnessed any of these miracles, nor do we have a way to know that they occurred at the time of their claims, except that people report about that, but reports do not yield knowledge, and the utmost in the matter is that they yield opinion (ẓann), but opinion is not considered valid in this matter. The sixth sect: a group of Sufis who say: occupation with other than God is a veil from knowledge of God Almighty, and the prophets call creation to acts of obedience and legal obligation, so they occupy creation with other than God and prevent them from occupation with God, so it must be that this is not true and correct. The seventh sect: those who say: we see the laws contain things in which there is no benefit, for fasting, prayer, and pilgrimage are acts that have no benefit for the worshipped, and they are harms and hardships for the worshippers, so that is a burden, rather foolishness, and that is not befitting the Most Wise of judges, so it must be that these laws are not from God. The eighth sect: those who affirm the principle of prophethood but dispute the prophethood of Muhammad.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a classification. Imam Razi lists eight groups (ṭawāʾif) that deny prophethood, each with a distinct argument. He begins with the first group, which rejects prophethood entirely on the grounds that the purpose of sending prophets is legal obligation (taklīf), but they consider taklīf false, so prophethood is false. The second group accepts taklīf but claims reason is sufficient, so prophets are unnecessary. The third group accepts the possibility of prophethood but denies that miracles prove it. The fourth group denies the possibility of miracles altogether. The fifth group asserts that we have no direct knowledge of past miracles, only reports, and reports only give opinion, which is insufficient. The sixth group, a sect of Sufis, claims that prophets distract people from God by calling them to acts of worship. The seventh group objects that religious laws contain useless hardships. The eighth group accepts prophethood in general but rejects Muhammad specifically. This enumeration sets the stage for Razi's subsequent refutations.

Key Terms
تكليف
عقل
حسن
قبح
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second group completely denies the need for any revelation.
Correction: They do accept taklīf but believe reason alone can guide it, making prophets unnecessary.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the second group regarding legal obligation?
A) Taklīf is false
B) Reason is sufficient for knowing taklīf
C) Miracles are impossible
D) Reports only give opinion

Key Takeaway
The second group denies prophethood by asserting that reason can judge good and evil without prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral decision, do you rely more on your own reasoning or on scriptural guidance? Can you think of a time your reason led you astray?
?
The group says for uncertain actions, avoid unless compelled. In your life, when you are unsure about a religious ruling, do you err on the side of caution or do you follow your own judgment? What does that say about your trust in revelation over reason?
?
If you truly believed reason is enough, how would your daily worship and seeking of prophetic guidance change? Are you willing to examine whether you secretly rely on your own reason in matters where the Quran and Sunnah have spoken?

Third Group: Miracles Not Proof
Razi presents the third group that denies miracles can prove a prophet's truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third group's claim that miracles do not serve as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Miracles can happen but don't prove a prophet is true.

The third group does not deny the possibility of prophethood or miracles, but they argue that miracles have no logical connection to the truth of a prophet's claim. This is a sophisticated epistemological objection that Razi must address.

Guided Path
1
Identify their concession
The third group accepts that sending prophets is rationally possible.
2
Find their key claim
They assert that the only possible proof of prophethood is miracles, but miracles provide no indication of truth.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since there is no valid proof, the claim of prophethood is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the third group denies the possibility of miracles.
Correction: They accept miracles but deny their evidential value for prophethood.

Checkpoint
What is the third group's objection to miracles as proof of prophethood?
A) Miracles are impossible
B) Miracles do not indicate messengership
C) Miracles are not reported reliably
D) Miracles are only for saints

Key Takeaway
The third group denies that miracles can serve as proof for prophethood, even though they accept their possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone told you that miracles have no logical connection to truthfulness, would you be able to explain why you believe the Prophet's miracles confirm his message? What would you rely on?
?
Have you ever considered that your faith might be based on something other than the miracles you've heard about? What is the real foundation of your certainty in the Prophet's truth?
?
The third group says that without proof, the claim of prophethood is invalid. If you had to prove to a skeptic that Muhammad is a true prophet using only reason, what would you say? Does your daily life reflect that conviction?

Fourth Group: Impossibility of Miracles
Razi presents the fourth group that denies the possibility of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth group's argument that miracles (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) are impossible.

Core Idea
If breaking natural laws is impossible, miracles cannot prove anything.

The fourth group takes a more radical stance: they argue that miraculous events (breaches of custom) are inherently impossible. This is a metaphysical position that denies the possibility of divine intervention that defies natural laws.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional premise
The fourth group says that if miracles were possible, they could prove a prophet, but they are impossible.
2
Why they think miracles are impossible
They claim that 'breaking of customs' (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) is impossible.
This is a philosophical claim about the nature of reality.
3
Conclusion
Since miracles are impossible, there is no proof for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth group might be confused with the third; both involve miracles.
Correction: The third group accepts miracles but denies their evidential value; the fourth denies miracles altogether.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the fourth group?
A) Miracles exist but don't prove prophethood
B) Miracles (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) are impossible
C) Reason is sufficient for moral knowledge
D) Reports of miracles are unreliable

Key Takeaway
The fourth group denies prophethood by asserting the impossibility of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah has the power to break every natural law at any moment, or is there a part of you that doubts miracles could really happen today?
?
If someone argued that miracles are impossible, would your iman (faith) be shaken? How would you respond using this lesson's passage?
?
When you read about the fourth group's denial, do you feel anger, pity, or something else? What does your reaction reveal about your own heart's conviction in Allah's unlimited power?

Prophethood
beginner

Fifth Group: Reports Only Give Opinion
Razi presents the fifth group that rejects prophethood due to insufficient certainty from reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth group's argument that historical reports of miracles yield only opinion (ẓann), not certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Testimony can't give certainty about past miracles.

The fifth group takes an empirical stance: we did not witness miracles ourselves, and reports from others only provide probable opinion, not certainty. Since certainty is required for religious belief, these reports are insufficient for establishing prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Notice their epistemological claim
The fifth group states that we have not seen miracles and cannot know they occurred.
2
Find their critique of testimony
They say that reports (khabar) do not yield knowledge (ʿilm) but only opinion (ẓann).
3
Understand why opinion is rejected
They assert that opinion is not considered valid in matters of prophethood.
This is a demand for certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن الذين ينكرون نبوة محمد طوائف : هذه الطائفة الأولى : الذين يقولون : المقصود من بعثة الأنبياء هو التكليف، ولكن القول بالتكليف باطل، فالقول ببعثة الأنبياء باطل. الطائفة الثانية : الذين يقولون : التكليف جائز إلا أن العقل كاف في معرفة التكليف ؛ لأن كل ما كان حسنا فعلناه وكل ما كان قبيحًا تركناه، وكل ما لا يمكننا أن نعرف حسنه وقبحه، فإن كنا مضطرين أو محتاجين إليه اكتفينا بالقدر الدافع للضرورة والحاجة، وإن لم تكن بنا إليه حاجة، امتنعنا عنه احترازًا عن الخطر. الطائفة الثالثة : الذين يقولون : البعثة جائزة في العقول، إلا أن الذي يمكن أن يجعل دليلا على كون الشخص رسولاً من عند الله ليس إلا المعجزات، وهذه المعجزات لا دلالة فيها البتة على الرسالة فلا جرم بطل القول ببعثة الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - لفقدان ما يدل عليها. الطائفة الرابعة الذين يقولون: لو أمكن حصول خوارق العادات لأمكن الاستدلال بها على صدق مدعي الرسالة، إلا أن انخراق العادات محال، فلا جرم لم يحصل ما يدل على صدقهم. الطائفة الخامسة : الذين يقولون نحن ما شاهدنا شيئًا من هذه المعجزات ولا طريق لنا إلى العلم بأنها حصلت وقت دعواهم، إلا أن الناس يخبرون عن ذلك غير أن الخبر لا يفيد العلم وأقصى ما في الباب أنه يفيد الظن، إلا أن الظن غير معتبر في هذا الباب. الطائفة السادسة: جمع من الصوفية يقولون: الاشتغال بغير الله حجاب عن معرفة الله تعالى، والأنبياء يدعون الخلق إلى الطاعات والتكليف، فهم يشغلون الخلق بغير الله، ويمنعونهم عن الاشتغال بالله فوجب أن لا يكون ذلك حقا وصدقاً. الطائفة السابعة : الذين يقولون : نرى الشرائع مشتملة على أشياء لا فائدة فيها، فإن الصوم والصلاة والحج أفعال لا منفعة فيها للمعبود، وهي مضار ومتاعب في حق العباد فكان ذلك عبئًا، بل سفها، وذلك لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين، فوجب أن لا تكون هذه الشرائع من عند الله. الطائفة الثامنة : الذين يسلمون أصل النبوة إلا أنهم ينازعون في نبوة محمد
Know that those who deny the prophethood of Muhammad are sects: This first sect: those who say: the purpose of sending prophets is legal obligation (taklīf), but the claim of legal obligation is false, so the claim of sending prophets is false. The second sect: those who say: legal obligation is permissible but reason is sufficient for knowing legal obligation; for everything that is good we do it, and everything that is evil we leave it, and everything whose goodness and evil we cannot know, if we are compelled or in need of it we suffice with the amount that repels necessity and need, and if we have no need of it, we refrain from it as a precaution against danger. The third sect: those who say: sending prophets is permissible in intellects, but that which can be made a proof that a person is a messenger from God is only miracles (muʿjizāt), and these miracles have no indication at all of messengership, so necessarily the claim of sending prophets—peace and blessings of God be upon them—is invalid due to the lack of what indicates it. The fourth sect: those who say: if the occurrence of extraordinary events (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) were possible, it would be possible to use them to prove the truthfulness of a claimant of messengership, but the breaking of customs is impossible, so necessarily there did not occur what indicates their truthfulness. The fifth sect: those who say: we have not witnessed any of these miracles, nor do we have a way to know that they occurred at the time of their claims, except that people report about that, but reports do not yield knowledge, and the utmost in the matter is that they yield opinion (ẓann), but opinion is not considered valid in this matter. The sixth sect: a group of Sufis who say: occupation with other than God is a veil from knowledge of God Almighty, and the prophets call creation to acts of obedience and legal obligation, so they occupy creation with other than God and prevent them from occupation with God, so it must be that this is not true and correct. The seventh sect: those who say: we see the laws contain things in which there is no benefit, for fasting, prayer, and pilgrimage are acts that have no benefit for the worshipped, and they are harms and hardships for the worshippers, so that is a burden, rather foolishness, and that is not befitting the Most Wise of judges, so it must be that these laws are not from God. The eighth sect: those who affirm the principle of prophethood but dispute the prophethood of Muhammad.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a classification. Imam Razi lists eight groups (ṭawāʾif) that deny prophethood, each with a distinct argument. He begins with the first group, which rejects prophethood entirely on the grounds that the purpose of sending prophets is legal obligation (taklīf), but they consider taklīf false, so prophethood is false. The second group accepts taklīf but claims reason is sufficient, so prophets are unnecessary. The third group accepts the possibility of prophethood but denies that miracles prove it. The fourth group denies the possibility of miracles altogether. The fifth group asserts that we have no direct knowledge of past miracles, only reports, and reports only give opinion, which is insufficient. The sixth group, a sect of Sufis, claims that prophets distract people from God by calling them to acts of worship. The seventh group objects that religious laws contain useless hardships. The eighth group accepts prophethood in general but rejects Muhammad specifically. This enumeration sets the stage for Razi's subsequent refutations.

Key Terms
ظن
علم
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fifth group might be thought to reject all historical knowledge.
Correction: They specifically reject the sufficiency of testimony for establishing prophethood, not all history.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth group, what do reports (khabar) yield?
A) Certain knowledge (ʿilm)
B) Opinion (ẓann)
C) Doubt
D) Direct experience

Key Takeaway
The fifth group denies prophethood by arguing that reports of miracles only give opinion, not certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The fifth group claims that reports only give opinion, not certainty. Do you personally base your belief in the prophethood of Muhammad (PBUH) on reports? If so, how do you know those reports give you certainty? Have you ever wrestled with this doubt?
?
If reports only provide opinion, then what is the foundation of your conviction in the unseen? Where do you truly derive your certainty from—not just intellectually, but in your heart?
?
Imagine you are faced with someone who sincerely holds this view. Would your own faith be shaken? What would you cling to that gives you unshakable certainty in the prophethood, despite not having witnessed the miracles yourself?

Prophethood

Sixth Group: Sufi Distraction
Razi presents the sixth group (Sufis) that argues prophets distract from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sufi group's argument that prophets distract people from God by calling them to religious obligations.

Core Idea
Worship obligations are a veil from God.

The sixth group comes from within the Islamic tradition—a group of Sufis who claim that engagement with religious duties (including those brought by prophets) distracts from direct knowledge of God. This is a spiritual argument against the very institution of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Understand their spiritual premise
The Sufi group states that occupation with anything other than God is a veil from knowledge of God.
2
Apply to prophets
They say prophets call people to acts of obedience and legal obligation, which occupy them with other than God.
3
Conclusion
Since prophets distract from God, their message cannot be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the sixth (Sufi) group?
A) Prophets perform miracles
B) Prophets distract people from God
C) Reason is sufficient
D) Miracles are impossible

Key Takeaway
The sixth group denies prophethood by claiming that prophetic calls to worship are a distraction from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Sufi group claims that even prophetic calls to worship can become a veil from God. Examine your own heart: have your daily prayers and religious duties ever become mere routines that feel distant from Allah? What would it take to transform them back into a means of drawing near?
?
If someone sincerely argued that your worship is distracting you from God, how would you respond? Would you be able to defend the prophetic guidance that calls you to these acts, or would you feel a sting of truth in their accusation?
?
What is one specific 'occupation with other than God' in your life—perhaps even a religious practice—that you suspect might be veiling you from a deeper knowledge of Allah? What concrete step will you take this week to remove that veil?

Seventh Group: Hardships Are Futile
Razi presents the seventh group that criticizes religious laws as futile burdens.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh group's argument that religious laws contain useless hardships and are therefore not from God.

Core Idea
If laws impose pointless hardship, they cannot be divine.

The seventh group objects to the content of religious laws themselves. They argue that acts like fasting, prayer, and pilgrimage are burdensome for humans and provide no benefit to God. Such futile impositions cannot be from a wise creator, so prophethood is false.

Guided Path
1
Identify their observation
They note that religious laws contain things with no apparent benefit.
2
Examples of hardship
They cite fasting, prayer, and pilgrimage as acts that harm the worshipper and do not benefit God.
3
Theological conclusion
Such pointless burden (safah) is unworthy of the All-Wise, so these laws cannot be from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This group might be mistaken as atheists, but they are using a theological argument about God's wisdom.
Correction: They argue from divine attributes—that a wise God would not impose useless hardships.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection of the seventh group?
A) Prophets did not perform miracles
B) Religious laws contain pointless hardships
C) Prophets distract from God
D) Reason is sufficient for moral knowledge

Key Takeaway
The seventh group denies prophethood by arguing that religious laws involve futile hardships incompatible with divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that certain acts of worship are just pointless routines? What does that thought reveal about your understanding of God's wisdom and your own faith? Will you commit to asking God for insight next time such a thought arises?
?
When you experience hardship in fulfilling religious obligations (like fasting or praying), do you ever feel resentful? How does the argument of the seventh group expose a danger in your heart, and what will you actively do to counter that feeling today?
?
If someone told you that religious duties are a burden and not from God, what in your personal experience would you point to as evidence of their deep benefit? Are you living in a way that shows you truly believe worship is not futile? What needs to change?

Prophethood

Eighth Group: Deny Muhammad's Prophethood
Razi presents the eighth group that singles out Muhammad for rejection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth group that accepts the principle of prophethood but denies that Muhammad is a prophet.

Core Idea
Prophethood in general is true, but Muhammad is not a prophet.

The eighth group differs from the previous seven: they do not deny prophethood altogether. Instead, they affirm that God sends prophets in general, but they reject the specific claim that Muhammad is a prophet. This is a common position of non-Muslim communities like Jews and Christians.

Guided Path
1
Understand their concession
The eighth group accepts the concept of prophethood in general.
2
Identify their denial
They specifically deny that Muhammad is a prophet.
This is the position of many religions that accept earlier prophets but not Muhammad.

اعلم أن الذين ينكرون نبوة محمد طوائف : هذه الطائفة الأولى : الذين يقولون : المقصود من بعثة الأنبياء هو التكليف، ولكن القول بالتكليف باطل، فالقول ببعثة الأنبياء باطل. الطائفة الثانية : الذين يقولون : التكليف جائز إلا أن العقل كاف في معرفة التكليف ؛ لأن كل ما كان حسنا فعلناه وكل ما كان قبيحًا تركناه، وكل ما لا يمكننا أن نعرف حسنه وقبحه، فإن كنا مضطرين أو محتاجين إليه اكتفينا بالقدر الدافع للضرورة والحاجة، وإن لم تكن بنا إليه حاجة، امتنعنا عنه احترازًا عن الخطر. الطائفة الثالثة : الذين يقولون : البعثة جائزة في العقول، إلا أن الذي يمكن أن يجعل دليلا على كون الشخص رسولاً من عند الله ليس إلا المعجزات، وهذه المعجزات لا دلالة فيها البتة على الرسالة فلا جرم بطل القول ببعثة الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - لفقدان ما يدل عليها. الطائفة الرابعة الذين يقولون: لو أمكن حصول خوارق العادات لأمكن الاستدلال بها على صدق مدعي الرسالة، إلا أن انخراق العادات محال، فلا جرم لم يحصل ما يدل على صدقهم. الطائفة الخامسة : الذين يقولون نحن ما شاهدنا شيئًا من هذه المعجزات ولا طريق لنا إلى العلم بأنها حصلت وقت دعواهم، إلا أن الناس يخبرون عن ذلك غير أن الخبر لا يفيد العلم وأقصى ما في الباب أنه يفيد الظن، إلا أن الظن غير معتبر في هذا الباب. الطائفة السادسة: جمع من الصوفية يقولون: الاشتغال بغير الله حجاب عن معرفة الله تعالى، والأنبياء يدعون الخلق إلى الطاعات والتكليف، فهم يشغلون الخلق بغير الله، ويمنعونهم عن الاشتغال بالله فوجب أن لا يكون ذلك حقا وصدقاً. الطائفة السابعة : الذين يقولون : نرى الشرائع مشتملة على أشياء لا فائدة فيها، فإن الصوم والصلاة والحج أفعال لا منفعة فيها للمعبود، وهي مضار ومتاعب في حق العباد فكان ذلك عبئًا، بل سفها، وذلك لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين، فوجب أن لا تكون هذه الشرائع من عند الله. الطائفة الثامنة : الذين يسلمون أصل النبوة إلا أنهم ينازعون في نبوة محمد
Know that those who deny the prophethood of Muhammad are sects: This first sect: those who say: the purpose of sending prophets is legal obligation (taklīf), but the claim of legal obligation is false, so the claim of sending prophets is false. The second sect: those who say: legal obligation is permissible but reason is sufficient for knowing legal obligation; for everything that is good we do it, and everything that is evil we leave it, and everything whose goodness and evil we cannot know, if we are compelled or in need of it we suffice with the amount that repels necessity and need, and if we have no need of it, we refrain from it as a precaution against danger. The third sect: those who say: sending prophets is permissible in intellects, but that which can be made a proof that a person is a messenger from God is only miracles (muʿjizāt), and these miracles have no indication at all of messengership, so necessarily the claim of sending prophets—peace and blessings of God be upon them—is invalid due to the lack of what indicates it. The fourth sect: those who say: if the occurrence of extraordinary events (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) were possible, it would be possible to use them to prove the truthfulness of a claimant of messengership, but the breaking of customs is impossible, so necessarily there did not occur what indicates their truthfulness. The fifth sect: those who say: we have not witnessed any of these miracles, nor do we have a way to know that they occurred at the time of their claims, except that people report about that, but reports do not yield knowledge, and the utmost in the matter is that they yield opinion (ẓann), but opinion is not considered valid in this matter. The sixth sect: a group of Sufis who say: occupation with other than God is a veil from knowledge of God Almighty, and the prophets call creation to acts of obedience and legal obligation, so they occupy creation with other than God and prevent them from occupation with God, so it must be that this is not true and correct. The seventh sect: those who say: we see the laws contain things in which there is no benefit, for fasting, prayer, and pilgrimage are acts that have no benefit for the worshipped, and they are harms and hardships for the worshippers, so that is a burden, rather foolishness, and that is not befitting the Most Wise of judges, so it must be that these laws are not from God. The eighth sect: those who affirm the principle of prophethood but dispute the prophethood of Muhammad.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a classification. Imam Razi lists eight groups (ṭawāʾif) that deny prophethood, each with a distinct argument. He begins with the first group, which rejects prophethood entirely on the grounds that the purpose of sending prophets is legal obligation (taklīf), but they consider taklīf false, so prophethood is false. The second group accepts taklīf but claims reason is sufficient, so prophets are unnecessary. The third group accepts the possibility of prophethood but denies that miracles prove it. The fourth group denies the possibility of miracles altogether. The fifth group asserts that we have no direct knowledge of past miracles, only reports, and reports only give opinion, which is insufficient. The sixth group, a sect of Sufis, claims that prophets distract people from God by calling them to acts of worship. The seventh group objects that religious laws contain useless hardships. The eighth group accepts prophethood in general but rejects Muhammad specifically. This enumeration sets the stage for Razi's subsequent refutations.

Key Terms
نبوة
محمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this group denies all prophethood, but they only deny Muhammad's.
Correction: They affirm the principle of prophethood (aṣl al-nubuwwa) but dispute Muhammad's claim.

Checkpoint
How does the eighth group differ from the first seven?
A) They deny all prophethood
B) They accept prophethood but deny Muhammad
C) They deny miracles
D) They rely on reason alone

Key Takeaway
The eighth group denies Muhammad's prophethood while accepting prophethood in general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone can accept prophethood in general yet deny Muhammad specifically, what does that reveal about the condition of their heart? Have you ever found yourself accepting a general truth of Islam while resisting its specific demand on your life?
?
Is there any aspect of following Muhammad (peace be upon him) that you find yourself disputing—perhaps not openly, but in your actions or priorities? What would it take to submit completely to his message today?
?
The eighth group stopped short of full submission. Where in your life are you tempted to do the same—to affirm a truth in theory but avoid its full implications? What is one step you can take today to move from general belief to specific obedience?

The Syllogism for Muhammad's Prophethood
Imam Razi presents the logical proof that Muhammad is a true prophet through a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's syllogistic proof that Muhammad is a true prophet and how it is supported by mutawatir reports.

Core Idea
A claim plus a confirming miracle equals true prophethood.

Imam Razi constructs a logical syllogism to prove Muhammad's prophethood. He shows that if a person claims prophethood and a miracle appears in support, that person is a true messenger. Since both conditions hold for Muhammad, the conclusion follows. Razi then begins to justify the first premise by appealing to mutawatir reports.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's structure
Read the passage and find the three key parts: the claim, the miracle, and the general rule that links them to the conclusion. Notice how Razi presents the conclusion first, then the premises.
The syllogism is categorical: All who claim and have a miracle are true; Muhammad claimed and had a miracle; therefore Muhammad is true.
2
Examine the first premise
Razi says the first premise 'Muhammad claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared' itself rests on further premises. Pay attention to the first sub-premise: Muhammad's claim is established by mutawatir reports.
Mutawatir means reports transmitted by so many people that certainty is achieved.
3
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why Razi uses a syllogism. He is demonstrating that proving Muhammad's prophethood also proves the principle of prophethood, not the reverse.
This avoids circularity.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify the conclusion of the syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا متى دللنا على صحة نبوة محمد فقد دللنا على صحة أصل النبوة، فنقول : إن محمدًا صلى الله عليه وسلم ادعى النبوة، وظهرت المعجزة على وفق دعواه، وكل من كان كذلك كان رسولا حقا ينتج أن محمدا رسول الله حقا. أما المقدمة الأولى وهي قولنا : أن محمدا ادعى النبوة، وظهرت المعجزة على وفق دعواه - فاعلم أن تقرير هذه المقدمة مبني على مقدمات : المقدمة الأولى : أن محمدا ادعى النبوة والاعتماد في إثبات هذه المقدمة على الأخبار المتواترة.
And know that when we demonstrate the soundness of Muhammad's prophethood, we have demonstrated the soundness of the principle of prophethood. So we say: Indeed Muhammad (peace be upon him) claimed prophethood, and the miracle appeared in accordance with his claim, and everyone who was like that is a true messenger, resulting in that Muhammad is truly the Messenger of God. As for the first premise, which is our statement: that Muhammad claimed prophethood, and the miracle appeared in accordance with his claim—know that the establishment of this premise is based on premises: The first premise: that Muhammad claimed prophethood, and reliance in establishing this premise is on mutawatir reports.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents the central proof for Muhammad's prophethood. It is a categorical syllogism with three terms: (1) Muhammad claimed prophethood, (2) a miracle appeared confirming his claim, (3) anyone with such a claim and confirming miracle is a true messenger. The conclusion: Muhammad is a true messenger. Then Razi begins to analyze the first premise, noting that its establishment depends on further premises, particularly the fact of Muhammad's claim, which is proven by mutawatir reports. This sets up the subsequent discussion on the definition and conditions of mutawatir.

Key Terms
نبوة
معجزة
مقدمة
خبر متواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes prophethood to prove prophethood.
Correction: Razi's syllogism does not assume prophethood; it uses the fact of a claim and a confirming miracle to deduce prophethood. The proof for the claim itself relies on mutawatir reports, which are independent.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the syllogism in this passage?
A) Muhammad claimed prophethood
B) A miracle appeared
C) Muhammad is truly the Messenger of God
D) Everyone claims prophethood

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi proves Muhammad's prophethood through a straightforward syllogism: a claim plus a confirming miracle equals a true prophet, and both conditions are met by Muhammad, established via mutawatir reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever personally examined the evidence for Muhammad's prophethood, or are you relying on the faith of your parents? This lesson provides a clear logical proof. Will you commit to knowing it so well that you can present it to others with conviction?
?
The proof depends on mutawatir reports—mass transmission of history. Do you truly trust that the story of Muhammad's claim and miracle has been preserved accurately? If so, does that trust translate into a certainty that shapes your daily decisions?
?
If someone challenged you today to prove that Muhammad is a true prophet, could you present this syllogism coherently? If not, what are you willing to do this week to internalize this proof until it becomes part of your own certainty?

Applying Mutawatir to Prove Muhammad's Prophethood
Razi applies the second type of mutawatir to establish Muhammad's claim to prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the second type of mutawatir report to establish certain knowledge of Muhammad's existence and his claim to prophethood.

Core Idea
Mutawatir reports can transmit claims, not just events.

Razi has already defined two types of mutawatir. Now he applies the second type—where a claim itself is reported through multiple chains—to the foundation of Islam: the prophethood of Muhammad. This lesson shows how a chain of transmission across generations yields certainty about a historical statement.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim
Read Razi's opening sentence where he states that knowledge of Muhammad's existence and his claim comes only through the second type of mutawatir.
2
Follow the chain
Notice how Razi describes the chain of transmission from our time back to the eyewitnesses. Each generation says they heard the previous generation.
3
Identify the conclusion
Understand that this chain produces certain knowledge, just as the earlier definition of the second type promised.
4
Reflect on significance
Ask yourself why this argument matters for proving prophethood. It shows that the claim itself is transmitted as a witnessed fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنما ثبت العلم بوجود محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وبكونه مدعيا للرسالة بالقسم الثاني من خبر التواتر. وتقريره : أنا سمعنا من أهل التواتر في عصرنا أنهم قالوا سمعنا أهل التواتر، وعلى هذا الترتيب نقل أهل التواتر، عن أهل التواتر إلى أن يصل هذا النقل إلى قوم قالوا : إنا شاهدنا محمدا بن عبد الله كان يقول : إني رسول الله إليكم، وقد عرفت أن مثل هذا الخبر يفيد العلم القطعي، فعلمنا بهذا الطريق أن محمدا كان موجودًا، وأنه كان يدعي أنه رسول الله.
And if you understand this, we say: Knowledge is only established of the existence of Muhammad, peace be upon him, and of his being a claimant to the message, through the second type of mutawatir report. Its elucidation: we have heard from the people of tawatur in our time that they said: we heard the people of tawatur, and in this order, the people of tawatur transmitted from the people of tawatur until this transmission reaches a people who said: we witnessed Muhammad ibn Abdallah saying: 'I am the Messenger of Allah to you.' And you have known that such a report benefits certain knowledge. So we have known by this path that Muhammad existed and that he claimed to be the Messenger of Allah.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a proof. After explaining the two types of mutawatir reports, the author now demonstrates how the second type applies to establish Muhammad's prophethood. He states that certain knowledge of Muhammad's existence and his claim to prophethood is obtained through the second type of mutawatir (transmission via a chain of narrators). The proof is: we in our time have heard from reliable transmitters who themselves heard from the previous generation, and so on up to the generation that witnessed Muhammad himself saying, 'I am the Messenger of Allah to you.' This chain meets the conditions of the second type of tawatur (each generation is numerous and reliable), so it yields certain knowledge. Therefore, we know with certainty that Muhammad existed and that he claimed prophethood. This argument is a key part of the proof of prophethood using transmitted reports.

Key Terms
القسم الثاني من خبر التواتر
مدعيا للرسالة
العلم القطعي
النقل
وجود محمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawatur only applies to direct sense perception, not to a claim like prophethood.
Correction: Razi shows that the claim itself (Muhammad saying 'I am the Messenger of Allah') is a perceptible statement that can be transmitted via mutawatir chains, thus yielding certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, how do we gain certain knowledge of Muhammad's claim to prophethood?
A) Through direct witnessing.
B) Through the second type of mutawatir report tracing back to eyewitnesses.
C) Through logical demonstration without need for reports.
D) Through a single reliable report.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves Muhammad's prophethood by showing that his claim is transmitted through a mutawatir chain, yielding certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Imagine you are one of the transmitters in this chain. Your generation hears the claim from the previous generation. How would you ensure that you faithfully transmit the exact claim to the next generation? What does this responsibility demand from your life right now?
?
When you say 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah,' you are repeating a claim that has been passed down for centuries. Does your daily life reflect that you truly believe this claim? If someone observed your actions, would they see a witness of that truth? What one change can you make starting today to align your life with this witness?

Two Types of Tawatur and the Proof of Prophethood
Imam al-Razi defines two types of tawatur and uses the second to prove prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of tawatur, their conditions, and how the second type establishes the prophethood of Muhammad (SAW) with certainty.

Core Idea
Tawatur transforms testimony into certain knowledge, and its second type proves prophethood.

Imam al-Razi begins by distinguishing reports that yield knowledge from those that do not, using the example of a sensible object. He then defines two types of tawatur — one based on direct sensory testimony and another based on an unbroken chain of transmission. Finally, he applies the second type to demonstrate the certainty of Muhammad's existence and his claim to prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and first definition
Start with the analogy about the West and 'Tam'aj' (a sensible object). Notice how the first type of tawatur requires two conditions: impossibility of collusion and a sensible reported thing.
The example shows why reports about metaphysical matters cannot reach certainty through tawatur.
2
Examine the second type of tawatur
Read the definition of the second type, where reporters say they heard from previous reporters. Note the additional condition: all levels must be equivalent in quantity and quality.
This type is like a chain of reliable transmitters.
3
Follow the application to prophethood
See how al-Razi applies the second type: from our generation back to the witnesses of Muhammad (SAW), each level reports hearing from the previous, ultimately establishing his existence and claim.
This demonstrates the practical use of tawatur in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the certainty achieved
Consider why al-Razi says such a report yields certain knowledge (علم يقيني). Think about the conditions that prevent doubt.
Certainty here is logical, not just probabilistic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغرب، لو أخبروا عن حدوث العالم ووحدة الصانع، لم يفد خبرهم العلم، أما إذا أخبروا عن وجود " طمعاج" أفاد خبرهم العلم ; لأن المخبر عنه شيء محسوس. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إذا حصل الشرطان - وهو أن يبلغ المخبرون في الكثرة إلى حيث يمتنع اتفاقهم على الكذب وكان المخبر عنه شيئًا محسوسًا - كان هذا الخبر مفيدًا للعلم اليقيني. القسم الثاني : أن يكون المخبرون في الكمية والكيفية بالوصف الذي ذكرناه إلا أنهم لا يقولون بأنا شاهدنا ذلك الشيء، بل يقولون : إنا سمعنا قوما موصوفين بالصفة المذكورة أنهم قالوا سمعنا أيضًا قوما موصوفين بالصفة المذكورة إنهم قالوا : شاهدنا الشيء الفلاني، فهذا القسم من الخبر أيضًا مفيد للعلم. ثالثا، والشرطان المعتبران في القسم الأول معتبران أيضًا ههنا إلا أن ههنا شرطا وهو أن يكون حال جميع الطبقات في الكمية والكيفية مساوية لحال الطبقة الأولى في الكيفية والكمية - على الوجه الذي ذكرناه. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنما ثبت العلم بوجود محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وبكونه مدعيا للرسالة بالقسم الثاني من خبر التواتر. وتقريره : أنا سمعنا من أهل التواتر في عصرنا أنهم قالوا سمعنا أهل التواتر، وعلى هذا الترتيب نقل أهل التواتر، عن أهل التواتر إلى أن يصل هذا النقل إلى قوم قالوا : إنا شاهدنا محمدا بن عبد الله كان يقول : إني رسول الله إليكم، وقد عرفت أن مثل هذا الخبر يفيد العلم القطعي، فعلمنا بهذا الطريق أن محمدا كان موجودًا، وأنه كان يدعي أنه رسول الله .
And the West, if they were to inform about the occurrence of the world and the oneness of the Creator, their report would not yield knowledge. But if they inform about the existence of 'Tam'aj', their report yields knowledge because the reported thing is sensible. If you understand this, we say: When two conditions are met—that the reporters reach a multiplicity such that it is impossible for them to agree upon a lie, and the reported thing is a sensible object—then this report yields certain knowledge. The second type: that the reporters are, in quantity and quality, as described, except that they do not say 'we witnessed that thing,' but rather say: 'We heard a group described with the mentioned description that they said: We also heard a group described with the mentioned description that they said: We witnessed such and such thing.' Thus this type of report also yields knowledge. Third, the two conditions considered in the first type are also considered here, but here there is an additional condition: that the state of all levels in quantity and quality is equivalent to the state of the first level in quality and quantity, in the manner we mentioned. If you understand this, we say: Knowledge of the existence of Muhammad, peace be upon him, and that he claimed prophethood is only established through the second type of tawatur report. Its elaboration: We heard from the people of tawatur in our time that they said: We heard the people of tawatur, and in this manner the people of tawatur transmitted from the people of tawatur until this transmission reaches a group who said: We witnessed Muhammad ibn Abdullah saying: 'I am the Messenger of God to you.' And you know that such a report yields certain knowledge. Thus we know through this path that Muhammad existed and that he claimed that he is the Messenger of God.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins with an analogy to illustrate the difference between reports of abstract matters and sensory matters. Then it defines the first type of tawatur: a report so numerous that collusion on falsehood is impossible and concerning a sensible object. Then it defines the second type: a chain of transmission where each level transmits from the previous level, with the additional condition that all levels are equivalent in quantity and quality. Finally, it applies this second type to establish the existence and claim of prophethood of Muhammad (SAW) through the unbroken chain of tawatur from the present generation back to the witnesses.

Key Terms
تواتر
خبر
علم يقيني
شرطان
محسوس
طبقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second type of tawatur is weaker than the first and does not yield certain knowledge.
Correction: Imam al-Razi affirms that the second type also yields certain knowledge, provided the additional condition of equivalence across all levels is met.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, what are the two conditions required for the first type of tawatur to yield certain knowledge?
A) The reporters must be many and the report must be about a metaphysical entity.
B) The reporters must be so numerous that collusion on a lie is impossible and the reported thing must be sensible.
C) The reporters must be trustworthy and the report must be about a historical event.
D) The reporters must be from different regions and the report must be widely known.

Key Takeaway
The second type of tawatur, with its chain of transmission and equivalence of levels, provides certain knowledge of the prophethood of Muhammad (SAW).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that the Prophet's message reaches you through a chain of tawatur. Do you truly respond to his call as if you had heard it directly from his blessed mouth? If not, what is holding you back?
?
Al-Razi says reports about abstract matters do not yield certainty. How certain are you about the unseen realities of faith (Paradise, Hell, accountability)? Have you built that certainty on something other than testimony—such as reflection on the signs of God?
?
The condition of equivalence across levels reminds us that every link in the chain matters. In your own transmission of Islamic knowledge to others, do you maintain the same care and truthfulness as those who came before you? What do you need to change starting today?
