How Miracles Confirm Prophethood: The King Analogy
Razi proves that a miracle following a claim confirms the claimant's truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument that a miracle following a prophetic claim serves as divine confirmation of truthfulness.

Core Idea
Miracles are divine confirmations that testify to a prophet's truthfulness.

This lesson explores a foundational argument in Islamic theology: that miracles prove the truthfulness of prophets. Razi presents a clear logical premise and illustrates it with a powerful analogy from kingship. By examining this reasoning, you will see how the splitting of the moon (the miracle) becomes a direct sign from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the source passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the logical flow from claim to proof.
Notice the structure: a restatement of the claim, then the third premise, then the proof through analogy.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
Break down the passage into: (1) the claim is established, (2) the third premise (miracle = truthfulness), (3) the proof: miracles are acts of God, created after the claim, indicating confirmation.
3
Analyze the king analogy
Understand how the king's extraordinary act represents the miracle and confirms the messenger. The king's action is not proof of the messenger's qualities but of his truthfulness.
The analogy is about actions, not essence.
4
Apply the analogy to the Prophet
See how the analogy applies to Muhammad's request for the moon to split. God's act of splitting the moon is like the king's extraordinary deed, confirming the Prophet's claim.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider what this means: the creation of the miracle in accordance with the claim is divine confirmation, and whomever Allah confirms is truthful.
This argument is foundational for establishing prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا أنه ادعى الرسالة وظهرت المعجزة على وفق دعواه. وأما المقدمة الثالثة - وهي أن كل من كان كذلك كان صادقًا - فالذي يدل عليه: هو أن المعجزات لما عجز عنها كان ذلك فعلا من أفعال الله تعالى خلقه في هذه عقيب دعواه، وخلق المعجزة عقيب الدعوى يدل على تصديق مدعي الرسالة. ومثاله: أنه إذا جلس الملك العظيم على سرير مملكته فقام واحد وقال: إني رسول هذا الملك إلى أهل مملكته ثم قال: يا أيها الملك إن كنت صادقًا في دعواي فافعل شيئًا يخالف عاداتك، فإذا فعل ذلك الملك في تلك الساعة فعلا يخالف عادته علم الحاضرون بالضرورة أنه إنما فعل ذلك لأجل تصديق ذلك المدعى، فهكذا ههنا أن محمدًا صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: يا أيها الناس إني رسول الله إليكم، ثم قال: يا إلهي إن كنت صادقًا في هذه الدعوى فاجعل القمر منشقًا بنصفين، فإذا انشق القمر علم كل واحد بالضرورة أنه تعالى إنما شقه بنصفين لأجل تصديقه، فثبت أن خلق المعجزة على وفق الدعوى تصديق من الله لذلك المدعى، وكل من صدقه الله تعالى فهو صادق.
And it is established by the totality of what we have mentioned that he claimed prophethood and the miracle appeared in accordance with his claim. As for the third premise – that everyone who is like that is truthful – what indicates it is: that miracles, since they are beyond ability, are an act of Allah, exalted, creating them at the time of his claim, and creating the miracle subsequent to the claim indicates the confirmation of the claimant of prophethood. Its analogy: when a great king sits on the throne of his kingdom, and a person stands up and says: I am the messenger of this king to his subjects, then says: O king, if you are truthful in my claim, then do something that contradicts your customs. Then if that king does at that moment an act that contradicts his custom, the bystanders know necessarily that he only did that for the sake of confirming that claimant. Similarly here: Muhammad ﷺ said: O people, I am the messenger of Allah to you, then said: O my God, if I am truthful in this claim, then split the moon into two halves. Then when the moon split, every single person knew necessarily that Allah only split it into two halves for the sake of confirming him. So it is established that the creation of the miracle in accordance with the claim is a confirmation from Allah of that claimant, and whomever Allah confirms is truthful.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the logical argument that a miracle proves the truthfulness of a prophet. Razi states that based on the evidence already given, it is established that the Prophet claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared. The third premise is that anyone for whom a miracle appears in accordance with his claim is truthful. The proof relies on the fact that a miracle is beyond human ability and is created by God at the time of the claim, which indicates divine confirmation. Razi provides a clear analogy: a king on his throne; a man claims to be his messenger and asks the king to do an extraordinary act. If the king does it, everyone knows he confirms the man. Similarly, the Prophet asked God to split the moon, and when it happened, everyone knew God confirmed him. Therefore, the miracle is a divine testimony to his truthfulness.

Key Terms
معجزة
تصديق
دعوى الرسالة
انشقاق القمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The king analogy implies God is like a human king with similar attributes.
Correction: The analogy only compares actions (king's extraordinary deed = miracle), not the nature of God. It is a logical parallel, not a theological equivalence.

Checkpoint
In the king analogy, what does the king's extraordinary act represent?
A) The miracle
B) The claim
C) The throne
D) The subjects

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that a miracle is a direct divine testimony to a prophet's truthfulness, and the moon-splitting incident exemplifies this reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the moon-splitting miracle, do you find yourself truly convinced that it was a divine confirmation of the Prophet's truthfulness, or do you harbor doubts? What would it cost you to surrender those doubts entirely?
?

?
The king analogy shows that a miraculous act in response to a claim confirms the messenger. Can you recall a time when you saw a clear answer to a prayer or a sign that you believed was from God? Did it strengthen your faith in the One who sent it? If not, what would it take for you to treat it as a divine confirmation?

Fourth Face: The Circularity of Gabriel's Infallibility
Razi presents the fourth face, showing circular dependency between Gabriel's infallibility and the Prophet's credibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical challenge of circular dependency between the Prophet's authority and Gabriel's infallibility in proving prophethood.

Core Idea
If Gabriel could be fallible, the Qur'an might be his deception, creating a circle that undermines proof of prophethood.

Razi now presents a clever epistemological objection: the proof of prophethood depends on Gabriel's infallibility, but that infallibility is known only through the Prophet — a vicious circle. This lesson unpacks that objection and shows why it threatens the standard proof from revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Focus on the logical flow.
The Arabic contains some uncertain phrases due to OCR; note them but don't get stuck.
2
Identify the two dependencies
Find where Razi says that the Prophet's prophethood depends on Gabriel's infallibility, and that Gabriel's infallibility depends on the Prophet's report.
Look for phrases like 'موقوف على' and 'لا طريق إلى'.
3
Understand why this is circular
Ask yourself: if each claim relies on the other, can either be proven without an independent basis? That is the definition of circular reasoning (dawr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أن محمدا لله كان يقول : جبريل اللي هو الذي يأتيني بهذا القرآن وهذا المعنى مذكور في القرآن، قال الله تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّهُ لَقَولُ رَسُولٍ كَريمِ ﴾ [الحَاقَّة : ٤٠] إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : بتقدير أن يقال: إن جبريل ال غير معصوم عن القبائح، لم يبعد أن يقال : إن هذا القرآن كلامه وأنه ألقاه على محمد الله القصد الإضلال والإغواء، وما لم يبطل هذا الاحتمال لم تثبت نبوة محمد الله فإن القول بصحة نبوته و موقوف على عصمة جبريل الة إلا أنه لا طريق إلى عصمة جبريل ال إلا بصحة نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فيتوقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر، فيكون دورًا.
The Fourth Face: That Muhammad (peace be upon him) used to say: 'Gabriel is the one who brings this Qur'an to me.' And this meaning is mentioned in the Qur'an: God Almighty said: 'Indeed, it is the word of a noble messenger' (Q. 69:40). If this is established, we say: On the assumption that it is said that Gabriel is not infallible from vile deeds, it is not far-fetched to say that this Qur'an is his speech and that he cast it upon Muhammad (peace be upon him) with the purpose of misleading and deluding. And as long as this possibility is not refuted, the prophethood of Muhammad is not established. For the affirmation of the validity of his prophethood is dependent upon the infallibility of Gabriel, but there is no way to [establish] the infallibility of Gabriel except through the validity of the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). So each of the two is dependent upon the other, resulting in circularity (dawr).
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth face (al-wajh al-rābi‘) of the objection. The opponent notes that the Prophet claims the Qur'an comes from Gabriel. The Qur'an itself states that it is the word of a noble messenger (Gabriel). If we entertain the possibility that Gabriel is not infallible (ma‘ṣūm), then he could have composed the Qur'an himself and delivered it to deceive people. To prove prophethood, we need to establish Gabriel's infallibility, but the only way to know Gabriel's infallibility is through the Prophet's report, which creates a vicious circle (dawr). Thus, the proof of prophethood is undermined by this circular dependency.

Key Terms
عصمة
جبريل
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection claims that Gabriel actually deceived the Prophet.
Correction: Razi is not asserting that Gabriel is fallible; he is only exploring a logical possibility to test the strength of the proof. The circle is an epistemological problem, not a factual claim.

Checkpoint
What circularity does the fourth face identify?
A) The Prophet's authority depends on Gabriel, and Gabriel's authority depends on the Prophet.
B) The Qur'an depends on the Prophet, and the Prophet depends on the Qur'an.
C) The miracles depend on God, and God depends on miracles.
D) The proof of God depends on the Prophet, and the Prophet depends on God.

Key Takeaway
The fourth face exposes a potential circularity in proving prophethood if Gabriel's infallibility is questioned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you ever pause to think that it could have been a lie? If not, why? How does the fact that God broke this circularity through miracles affect your daily reliance on the Qur'an?
?
The dependency between the Prophet's word and Gabriel's infallibility is a logical circle. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that rest on a similar circular trust? How do you break that circle?
?
If you were to question the infallibility of Gabriel, your entire faith would be at risk. Take a moment to examine any doubts you have about the revelation. Can you identify a reason—beyond the circular argument—that confirms to you that the Qur'an is from God?

The Sabian Objection to Miracles as Proof
Razi presents the Sabian objection about planetary messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sabian objection that miracles could be attributed to planets, and why this doubt must be removed to establish prophethood.

Core Idea
If planets are rational and communicate, they could explain miracles.

This lesson presents the fifth face of the objection, drawing on the Sabian doctrine that celestial bodies are rational and communicative. If planets can speak to humans, they could also send a messenger, casting doubt on whether miracles truly come from God. Understanding this objection is essential to seeing why the proof of prophethood requires eliminating alternative explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the Sabian claim about living planets and their potential to send messengers.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the conditional: if planets are living and speak, then they could choose a messenger and perform miracles, undermining the proof.
Razi presents this as a hypothetical that needs refutation.
3
Recognize the need for refutation
Razi states that until this possibility is invalidated, doubt remains. Understand why refuting it is crucial for certainty.
This sets up the next lesson where Razi likely answers the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أن مذهب الصابئة أن الأفلاك والكواكب أحياء ناطقة وأن هذه الكواكب قد تتكلم مع بعض الناس وإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : بعض الكواكب يختار من الناس شخصًا واحدًا ويبعثه إلى الخلق على سبيل الرسالة، وقبل إبطال هذا الاحتمال في الدليل كان الشك قائمًا ، فلعل مظهر هذه المعجزات هو الكواكب المخصوصة.
The Fifth Face: That the doctrine of the Sabians is that the celestial spheres and the planets are living rational beings, and that these planets may speak with some people. If that is allowed, then why is it not permissible to say that some planets choose one person from among people and send him to the creation as a messenger? And until this possibility is invalidated in the proof, doubt remains. Perhaps the manifestor of these miracles is the specific planets.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth face (al-wajh al-khāmis) of the objection, drawing on the Sabian school of thought. The Sabians believed that celestial bodies are living rational beings capable of communication. If that is possible, then a planet could select a person and send him as a messenger, and the miracles associated with that person could be the work of that planet rather than God. Until this possibility is refuted, the proof of prophethood from miracles is not certain.

Key Terms
الصابئة
الأفلاك
الكواكب
أحياء ناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sabian view is presented as a valid alternative to prophethood.
Correction: Razi presents the Sabian view as an objection that must be refuted, not as an accepted doctrine.

Checkpoint
What do the Sabians believe according to the passage?
A) God creates miracles directly.
B) Planets are living rational beings that can speak to humans.
C) Angels are the only messengers.
D) Miracles are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Sabian belief in living planets offers an alternative explanation for miracles, so the proof of prophethood must eliminate such possibilities to be certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If a clear miracle occurred before you, would you be tempted to explain it away by science or natural causes? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own heart for resistances to divine proofs?
?
Razi says that until the Sabian possibility is invalidated, doubt remains. What possible doubts about Allah's guidance have you allowed to linger without seeking their refutation? Are you willing to actively seek answers until that doubt is removed?

Refutation of the Devil-Input Objection
Razi refutes the devil-input objection with two counter-objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that prophets' unseen knowledge came from devils by exposing logical self-contradictions in the objection.

Core Idea
A valid objection must not undermine its own foundations.

Imam Razi presents a common objection against the proof of prophethood through miracles: perhaps the prophets' knowledge of the unseen came from devils. He then demolishes this objection by showing that it leads to absurdities. This passage demonstrates the method of refuting objections by exposing internal contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection (first sentence) and then the two counter-objections introduced by 'لا يقال'.
The objector is anticipating possible rebuttals.
2
Understand the first counter-objection and answer
The first counter-objection: prophets cursed devils, so why would devils help them? Razi answers: the devil tolerates the curse if it leads to greater misguidance.
Consider the devil's motive.
3
Understand the second counter-objection and answer
The second counter-objection: we only know of jinn and devils from prophets, so using them against prophecy is self-refuting. Razi answers: we don't need certainty about jinn and devils; mere possibility suffices to raise doubt.
This answer shifts the burden of proof.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Razi's refutation shows that the objection either contradicts what prophets teach about devils or relies on the very prophecy it seeks to challenge.
The objection is self-undermining.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولعل الإخبار عن الغيوب حصل بإلقاء الشياطين تلك الغيوب إليهم.
لا يقال : هذا باطل من وجهين :
الأول : أن الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلمه عليهم - دعوا الخلق إلى لعن الشياطين، فكيف يليق بالشياطين أن يعينوهم ؟
والثاني: أنا إنما نثبت الجن والشياطين بإخبار الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلمه عليهم - فلو جعلنا القول بالجن والشياطين طاعنا في النبوة كنا قد أبطلنا الأصل بالفرع، وذلك باطل.
لأنا نجيب عن الأول بأن الشيطان إذا كان مشغوفًا بإضلال الخلق - وهذا الطريق يفضي إلى حصول مقصوده - لم يبعد أن يرضى بذلك اللعن ليحصل هذا المقصود له.
ونجيب عن الثاني : أنا لا نحتاج في مقام السؤال إلى الجزم بوجود الجن والشياطين، بل يكفينا مجرد الاحتمال ومجرد الاحتمال لا يتوقف القول بصحة النبوة.
And perhaps the reporting of unseen matters occurred through the devils casting those unseen things to them. It cannot be said: This is false on two counts: First, the prophets – God's blessings and peace upon them – called people to curse the devils, so how could it befit the devils to assist them? Second, we only affirm jinn and devils on the basis of the reports of the prophets – God's blessings and peace upon them – so if we make belief in jinn and devils an accusation against prophethood, we would have nullified the principle by the branch, and that is false. We answer the first by saying: When the devil is obsessed with leading people astray – and this path leads to achieving his objective – it is not improbable that he would tolerate that curse to attain this objective. And we answer the second: At the stage of inquiry we do not need to be certain of the existence of jinn and devils; rather, mere possibility suffices, and mere possibility does not prevent affirming the truth of prophethood.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the proof of prophethood through miracles and then refutes it. The objector suggests that the prophets' knowledge of the unseen could have come from devils rather than divine revelation. Razi first states the objection, then raises two counter-objections against it (the 'لا يقال' is actually the objector anticipating those counter-objections) and then provides the answers. The first counter-objection is that the prophets called people to curse the devils, so why would devils help them? The answer is that the devil might tolerate the curse if it leads to greater misguidance. The second counter-objection is that we only know of jinn and devils from the prophets themselves, so using them to impugn prophecy would be to undermine the source. The answer is that we do not need certainty about jinn and devils; mere possibility suffices to raise doubt. Razi's point is that this objection fails because the objector's premises are self-undermining.

Key Terms
الشياطين
الجن
النبوة
المعجزات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that devils could have given prophets knowledge of the unseen is a valid challenge to prophethood.
Correction: Razi shows that the objection is self-defeating because it either contradicts what prophets teach about devils or relies on the very prophetic reports it seeks to undermine.

Checkpoint
What is the first counter-objection against the claim that devils gave the prophets knowledge of the unseen?
A) The prophets were too powerful to be influenced by devils.
B) The prophets themselves cursed the devils, so devils would not assist them.
C) The devils are not capable of knowing the unseen.
D) The devils were created by God for that purpose.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the devil-input objection by showing that its premises either contradict prophetic teachings or rely on the very prophecy they seek to undermine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever let a mere possibility—something that could be true but has no evidence—shake your certainty in a core belief about Allah or His messengers? What would it take for you to respond like Razi, refusing to let speculation undermine what you know to be true?
?
Razi shows that the devil might tolerate being cursed if it leads to greater misguidance. Think of a time you were tempted to do something wrong because it seemed to bring a short-term good. Did you stop to consider whether the long-term spiritual harm outweighed the fleeting benefit? How can you guard against such devilish calculus?
?
If you had to defend the truth of prophethood to someone who raised the devil-input objection, would you be able to articulate Razi's logical response? If not, what does that say about your readiness to stand firm in your belief when challenged? Will you take the time to truly understand these arguments so they become your own?

Objection: Jibreel's Infallibility Creates Circularity
Razi presents the objection that proving Jibreel's infallibility depends on prophecy, creating a circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection to the proof of prophethood: that proving Jibreel's infallibility leads to a vicious circle when used to establish Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
A vicious circle threatens the proof of prophethood when Jibreel's infallibility depends on what it is meant to prove.

Razi presents a fourth objection against the standard proof of prophethood based on the Qur'an. The objector argues that to accept the Qur'an as revelation from God, we must assume the angel Jibreel is infallible. But the only way to prove Jibreel's infallibility is through the prophethood of Muhammad, creating a logical circle. This objection challenges the very foundation of relying on revelation to prove prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
This is the fourth aspect of objections to prophethood proofs. The objection targets the reliance on Jibreel as the conveyor of revelation.
The objection is part of a series, each targeting different assumptions.
2
Identify the two premises
Muhammad claimed Jibreel brought the Qur'an, and the Qur'an itself (Surah al-Haaqqa:40) affirms that the Qur'an is the speech of a noble messenger (Jibreel).
The verse is used to support the claim that Jibreel is the messenger.
3
Recognize the circular reasoning
The objector shows that if Jibreel is not infallible, the Qur'an could be his deception. To prove Muhammad's prophethood, we need Jibreel's infallibility. But the only way to prove Jibreel's infallibility is through the truth of Muhammad's prophethood. Thus each depends on the other, forming a vicious circle.
This is a classic circularity argument (dawr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أن محمدا لله كان يقول : جبريل اللي هو الذي يأتيني بهذا القرآن وهذا المعنى مذكور في القرآن، قال الله تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّهُ لَقَولُ رَسُولٍ كَريمِ ﴾ [الحَاقَّة : ٤٠] إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : بتقدير أن يقال: إن جبريل ال غير معصوم عن القبائح، لم يبعد أن يقال : إن هذا القرآن كلامه وأنه ألقاه على محمد الله القصد الإضلال والإغواء، وما لم يبطل هذا الاحتمال لم تثبت نبوة محمد الله فإن القول بصحة نبوته و موقوف على عصمة جبريل الة إلا أنه لا طريق إلى عصمة جبريل ال إلا بصحة نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فيتوقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر، فيكون دورًا.
The fourth aspect: That Muhammad (peace be upon him? [text has 'Muhammadan lillah']) used to say: Jibreel (?) is the one who brings me this Qur'an, and this meaning is mentioned in the Qur'an. Allah said: {Verily it is the speech of a noble messenger} [Surah al-Haaqqa: 40]. If this is established, we say: Supposing it is said that Jibreel (?) is not infallible from evil deeds, it is not unlikely to say that this Qur'an is his speech and that he cast it upon Muhammad (?) with the intent of misguidance and leading astray. As long as this probability is not nullified, the prophethood of Muhammad (?) is not established. For the claim of the truth of his prophethood depends on the infallibility of Jibreel (?), yet there is no way to the infallibility of Jibreel (?) except through the truth of the prophethood of Muhammad (peace be upon him). Thus each depends on the other, resulting in a vicious circle.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection against the proof of prophethood: a circularity argument involving the angel Jibreel. The objector says: Muhammad claimed that Jibreel brought him the Qur'an, and the Qur'an itself says it is the speech of a noble messenger (Jibreel). If we suppose Jibreel is not infallible, then the Qur'an could be his own speech intended to mislead. To establish Muhammad's prophethood, we need Jibreel's infallibility. But the only way to prove Jibreel's infallibility is through the truth of Muhammad's prophethood (since the Qur'an asserts Jibreel's nobility). Thus each depends on the other, forming a vicious circle. This objection attacks the logical coherence of the proof from revelation.

Key Terms
عصمة (isma)
جبريل (Jibreel)
دور (dawr)
النبوة (nubuwwa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jibreel's infallibility is obvious from the Qur'an and does not need proof.
Correction: The objector argues that the very assumption of Jibreel's infallibility creates a circular dependency when trying to prove prophethood, since the Qur'an (which asserts his nobility) is only authoritative if Muhammad is a true prophet.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, what creates a circular dependency?
A) The prophethood of Muhammad depends on Jibreel's infallibility, and Jibreel's infallibility depends on Muhammad's prophethood.
B) The miracles depend on God, and God's existence depends on miracles.
C) The Qur'an proves Muhammad's prophethood, and Muhammad proves the Qur'an.
D) The existence of jinn depends on prophecy, and prophecy depends on jinn.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection shows that using the Qur'an to prove prophethood can lead to a vicious circle if the angel's infallibility is not independently established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone presented you with this circularity argument, would you be able to respond confidently? What might that reveal about the foundation of your faith?
?
The objection forces us to examine the basis of our trust in revelation. In your own life, do you rely on circular reasoning when defending your beliefs? Be honest: what is your ultimate ground for certainty?
?
This challenge questions whether the Qur'an can independently prove prophethood. Has your own conviction in Muhammad's prophethood ever been tested by such logical scrutiny? How did you respond?

The Sabian Objection to Miracles
Razi presents the Sabian belief as a fifth objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sabian belief in speaking planets presents a rational objection to miracles as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Alternative explanations challenge the evidence for miracles.

This lesson examines an objection raised by those who attribute miraculous events to planetary powers rather than to God. The Sabian belief offers a naturalistic alternative that must be refuted before miracles can be used as proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Notice the logical structure: Razi states the Sabian doctrine, then asks why it cannot explain miracles.
Pay attention to the word 'لعل' (perhaps) which introduces the alternative explanation.
2
Identify the core claim
Find where Razi says the planets are 'أحياء ناطقة' (living, speaking). This is the basis of the objection.
3
Understand the logical challenge
Razi argues that if planets can speak to people, then a planet could choose a messenger. This would make miracles attributable to the planet, not God. Doubt remains until this is refuted.
This is a conditional argument: if P (planets speak), then Q (they could send messengers).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is important: it forces us to prove that miracles come only from God, not from any other being.
The Sabian view is an example of a 'competing hypothesis' that must be eliminated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : أن مذهب الصابئة أن الأفلاك والكواكب أحياء ناطقة وأن هذه الكواكب قد تتكلم مع بعض الناس وإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : بعض الكواكب يختار من الناس شخصًا واحدًا ويبعثه إلى الخلق على سبيل الرسالة، وقبل إبطال هذا الاحتمال في الدليل كان الشك قائمًا ، فلعل مظهر هذه المعجزات هو الكواكب المخصوصة.
The fifth aspect: That the doctrine of the Sabians is that the celestial spheres and planets are living, speaking beings, and that these planets may speak to some people. If that is permissible, then why is it not permissible to say that some planet selects a single person from among people and sends him to creation as a messenger? Until this probability is invalidated in the proof, doubt remains. Perhaps the manifestor of these miracles is that particular planet.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth objection: the Sabian belief that planets are living and can speak. If that were true, then miracles could be performed by a planet choosing a person as its messenger. As long as this possibility is not refuted, one cannot be certain that the miracle is from God. The objector thus challenges the necessity of attributing miracles to God.

Key Terms
الصابئة (Sabians)
الكواكب (kawakib)
الأفلاك (aflak)
المعجزات (mu'jizat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sabian beliefs are irrelevant and easily dismissed.
Correction: The objection shows that alternative explanations must be excluded before a miracle can be attributed to God.

Checkpoint
What do the Sabians believe about the planets according to this passage?
A) They are inanimate bodies that influence events.
B) They are living, speaking beings that can communicate with people.
C) They are gods that created the world.
D) They are angels that carry divine messages.

Key Takeaway
The Sabian objection forces us to prove that only God, not any planet, can be the true source of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute the Prophet's miracles to Allah, have you personally examined and eliminated every alternative explanation—including the Sabian one—or are you relying on inherited certainty? What would it take for you to replace that with conviction built on evidence?
?
Imagine you encountered a miracle today that seemed undeniable. How would you test whether it came from Allah or from some other power? This lesson forces you to admit that your first assumption might not be enough. Are you willing to adopt a method that rules out every possibility before you claim certainty?
?
The Sabian objection is ancient, but the doubt it raises is timeless: your faith must be able to withstand the toughest counterarguments. Have you ever honestly confronted the possibility that the Quran could be from other than Allah? If not, what holds you back? Commit today to studying the proofs until no doubt remains.

Objection: Could Celestial Souls Produce Miracles?
Razi presents the seventh objection: philosophers attribute intellects and souls to celestial bodies, which could manifest miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that celestial intellects and souls might be the agents behind miracles, challenging the necessity of attributing them directly to God.

Core Idea
Miracles might not require divine intervention if celestial souls can do them.

This lesson introduces a key objection from philosophers: if celestial spheres and planets have abstract intellects and souls, why couldn't they produce extraordinary events? This challenges the Islamic argument that miracles necessarily come from God as proof of prophethood. Let's examine how Razi presents this challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice it is a rhetorical question: 'Why is it not permissible...?'
2
Identify the philosophical claim
The philosophers affirm three types of entities for celestial bodies: abstract intellects, rational souls, and animal souls. Understand each.
3
Consider the implication for miracles
If these entities can manifest extraordinary events, then miracles are not exclusive indicators of prophethood. This undermines the proof.
This is a serious challenge that requires a response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع : أن الفلاسفة يثبتون للأفلاك والكواكب عقولاً مجردة، ونفوسا ناطقة ونفوسا حيوانية فلكية فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المظهر لهذه الخوارق شيئًا من هذه الأشياء عقلا أو نفسا؟
The seventh aspect: That the philosophers affirm for the spheres and planets abstract intellects, rational souls, and animal souls of the spheres. So why is it not permissible that the manifestor of these extraordinary events be something among these things – an intellect or a soul?
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a seventh objection: philosophers posit that the celestial spheres and planets have intellects and souls. These immaterial beings might have the power to produce extraordinary events (kharq al-'ada). If that is possible, then miracles need not be directly from God; they could be the work of a celestial intellect or soul. This further undermines the necessity of attributing miracles to God as proof of prophethood.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة (falasifa)
عقول مجردة (uqul mujarrada)
نفوس ناطقة (nufus natiqa)
خوارق (khawariq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that only God can produce miracles, but the objector argues that even lesser beings like celestial intellects could do so.
Correction: The objection is a philosophical challenge; Islamic theology maintains that miracles are divine acts that surpass the capacity of created beings, including celestial intellects.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh objection, what do philosophers affirm for the spheres and planets?
A) They are inanimate and subject to natural laws.
B) They have abstract intellects, rational souls, and animal souls.
C) They are controlled by angels.
D) They are gods worthy of worship.

Key Takeaway
The philosophical objection that celestial souls could manifest miracles challenges the exclusivity of miracles as divine proof of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Razi's objection, does the possibility that miracles could be caused by celestial souls challenge your certainty that they are direct signs from Allah? How does this lesson affect your readiness to defend prophethood against philosophical objections that seem plausible?

Objection: Astrological Conjunctions Could Explain Miracles
Razi presents the astrologers' objection about celestial conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astrological objection that planetary conjunctions could be mistaken for miracles, and why this challenges the evidentiary value of miracles.

Core Idea
Could a false prophet simply time his claim with a rare planetary conjunction?

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a sixth objection from the astrologers: that extraordinary events attributed to miracles could actually be caused by celestial conjunctions. A clever claimant could wait for a predicted conjunction and then claim prophethood, leading people to believe his claim is divinely supported. This objection seeks to undermine the evidential force of miracles by attributing them to natural astronomical phenomena.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the structure of the objection.
The passage is a single argument with several steps.
2
Identify the astrologers' claim
The objector starts by stating that astrologers agree on the effects of planetary conjunctions. This is the premise.
The key term is 'اتصالات الكواكب' (conjunctions of planets).
3
Understand the false prophet's strategy
The objection describes a scenario: a knowledgeable person predicts a conjunction, waits for its time, claims prophethood, and when the effect occurs, people mistake it for a miracle.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Consider the challenge to miracles
Reflect on how this objection tries to explain away miracles as natural events. It questions whether miracles can be definitive proof of prophethood if they can be faked through knowledge of astronomy.
The objection is not necessarily endorsed by Razi; he is presenting it to refute it later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : أن المنجمين اتفقوا على أن لاتصالات الكواكب آثارًا عجيبة في هذا العالم فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الرسول الله لو كان أعلم بالنجوم، فعلم سيحدث اتصال عجيب في الفلك وله تأثير عجيب في هذا العالم، فصبر حتى جاء وقت ظهور ذلك التأثير وادعى النبوة في ذلك الوقت، فوقع ذلك الأثر العجيب على وفق دعواه فظن الناس أنه معجز خلقه الله تعالى، وما كان الأمر كذلك.
The sixth aspect: That the astrologers agree that conjunctions of the planets have wondrous effects in this world. So why is it not permissible to say that the Messenger of God (?) if he were more knowledgeable of the stars, and knew that a wondrous conjunction would occur in the celestial sphere having a wondrous effect in this world, and he waited until the time of the appearance of that effect came and claimed prophethood at that time, then that wondrous effect occurred in accordance with his claim, so people thought it was a miracle created by God Almighty, but the matter was not so.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a sixth objection: astrologers believe planetary conjunctions have powerful effects. If a claimant to prophethood were well-versed in astrology and could predict a major conjunction, he could time his claim to coincide with that conjunction's effects. People would then mistake the natural effect for a miracle. Thus, miracles could be explained by natural celestial events rather than divine intervention.

Key Terms
المنجمين (munajjimun)
اتصالات الكواكب (ittisalat al-kawakib)
المعجزات (mu'jizat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are always supernatural and cannot be explained by natural causes.
Correction: This objection suggests that some apparent miracles could be natural events like planetary conjunctions, challenging the assumption that all miracles are divine interventions.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth objection, how could a false prophet produce something that appears to be a miracle?
A) By using magic taught by devils.
B) By predicting a rare planetary conjunction and claiming it as his miracle.
C) By performing tricks through secret knowledge.
D) By appealing to the common belief in jinn.

Key Takeaway
The astrological objection attempts to reduce miracles to predictable natural phenomena, challenging their role as divine confirmation of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you give more weight to signs from nature (like coincidences or 'lucky' events) than to clear divine guidance? What would it take for you to recognize a true sign from God?
?

?

The Eighth Objection: Astrological Lots as an Alternative Explanation for Prophecy
Razi presents the astrologers' claim that certain lots grant power and knowledge of the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the astrologers' claim about astrological lots poses a skeptical challenge to the certainty that miracles are from God, and the overall conclusion drawn from the eight objections.

Core Idea
Even a false astrological claim can cast enough doubt to block certainty about miracles.

This lesson presents the final objection in Razi's series, where he considers the astrologers' argument that two astrological 'lots'—Sahm al-Sa'ada and Sahm al-Ghayb—could empower prophets to perform miracles and report the unseen. Although Razi notes that the astrologers are wrong, the mere possibility raises a skeptical challenge. The passage then concludes that through these eight objections, one cannot be certain that miracles come from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is structured: the astrologers' claim, the author's caveat, and the conclusion.
The Arabic uses the phrase 'الوجه الثامن' to introduce the eighth aspect.
2
Identify the two astrological lots
Find the two lots mentioned: 'سهم السعادة' (Lot of Fortune) and 'سهم الغيب' (Lot of the Unseen). Note their claimed effects: strengthening a person in achieving goals and enabling reports of the unseen.
These are technical astrological terms from the Hellenistic tradition.
3
Understand the skeptical logic
Razi says even though we know the astrologers are wrong, the possibility remains. If their claim were true, then miracles and prophecy could be explained by astrology, not divine intervention. This undermines certainty.
The phrase 'الاحتمال قائم' indicates that the mere possibility is enough for the objection.
4
Grasp the overall conclusion
The passage ends by stating that through these eight objections, it is impossible to be certain that the agent of miracles is God. This challenges the foundational proof of prophethood.
This conclusion ties together all eight objections, not just this one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثامن : المنجمون اتفقوا على أن لسهم السعادة أثرا قويًا في تقوية الإنسان على تحصيل المرادات والسعادات ولسهم الغيب أثرًا قويًا في كون الإنسان متمكنا الإخبار عن المغيبات، ونحن وإن كنا نعلم بالدليل عدم صدقهم في هذه المقالة إلا أن الاحتمال قائم وبتقدير أن يكون ما قالوه حقا لم يمكننا أن نقطع بأن ظهور المعجزات الله تعالى ولا بأن نقطع بأن إخبار الأنبياء عن المغيبات بوحي الله تعالى، بل جاز من أن يكون بقوة هذين السهمين في طوالعهم.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثمانية أنه لا يمكن القطع بأن فاعل هذه المعجزات هو الله تعالى.
The eighth aspect: The astrologers agreed that 'Sahm al-Sa'ada' (Lot of Fortune) has a strong effect in strengthening a person in acquiring objectives and felicities, and 'Sahm al-Ghayb' (Lot of the Unseen) has a strong effect in a person being able to report unseen matters. And though we know by proof that they are not truthful in this statement, yet the possibility remains. And supposing what they said were true, we could not be certain that the appearance of miracles is from God Almighty, nor could we be certain that the prophets' reports of the unseen are by revelation from God Almighty; rather, it could be through the power of these two lots in their horoscopes. Thus, by these eight aspects, it is established that it is impossible to be certain that the agent of these miracles is God Almighty.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth and final objection in this set, then draws the overall conclusion. The astrologers claim that two 'lots' (astrological points) – Sahm al-Sa'ada (Lot of Fortune) and Sahm al-Ghayb (Lot of the Unseen) – have powerful effects: one strengthens a person in achieving goals, the other gives knowledge of the unseen. Even though we know the astrologers are wrong, the mere possibility suffices to cast doubt. If their claim were true, then prophetic miracles and knowledge of the unseen could be attributed to astrological influences rather than divine revelation. The author then concludes that through these eight objections, one cannot be certain that miracles are performed by God, thus challenging the standard proof of prophethood.

Key Terms
سهم السعادة (sahm al-sa'ada)
سهم الغيب (sahm al-ghayb)
المغيبات (mughayyabat)
المعجزات (mu'jizat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only God can grant knowledge of the unseen, so prophetic knowledge must be from God.
Correction: The objector suggests that astrological means (like the Lot of the Unseen) could be an alternative source, thus undermining the certainty that such knowledge is exclusively from God.

Checkpoint
What is the overall conclusion drawn from the eight aspects (wujuh)?
A) That miracles are definitely from God.
B) That it is impossible to be certain that miracles are from God based on these objections.
C) That the prophets were astrologers.
D) That the Sabians are correct.

Key Takeaway
The eighth objection introduces astrological lots as a hypothetical alternative explanation for miracles and prophetic knowledge, concluding that such possibilities prevent absolute certainty in divine agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any claims or ideas you have accepted that, even if false according to you, create a lingering doubt about your certainty in Allah's signs? How will you confront that doubt today?
?
When you read Razi's argument, do you feel that you have ever allowed a mere possibility to weaken your conviction in the truth of prophethood? What step will you take to strengthen that conviction?
?
If someone told you that the Quran's miracles could be explained by natural causes, would you be able to respond with certainty, or would a seed of doubt grow? In what area of your faith do you need to build a stronger foundation against such possibilities?

Third Objection: Miracles Cannot Prove Truthfulness
Razi formulates a two-part objection against the purposive explanation of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-part objection against using miracles as proof of prophethood, and how it challenges the link between miracles and divine confirmation.

Core Idea
Miracles might mislead; purpose-driven action leads to determinism.

Razi continues his critique of using miracles to verify prophethood. Even if we grant that miracles come from God, why assume they are meant to confirm truth? This objection dismantles that assumption by presenting two powerful arguments: one from God's transcendence of purposes, and another from the dilemma of motive-dependence. Let's follow Razi's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its two aspects
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the objection has two clearly marked 'aspects' (وجهين).
Pay attention to the logical flow: the first aspect relies on a prior theological principle.
2
Understand the first aspect: God's actions are not purposive
The first aspect argues that since you (the opponent) have proven God's actions are not motivated by purposes, you cannot claim miracles are for confirmation.
3
Analyze the second aspect: the dilemma of motive (da'ii)
The second aspect presents a dilemma: either acts depend on a motive or they don't. If they do, determinism follows and God could create miracles to mislead. If they don't, miracles could be purposeless and thus not evidence of truth.
This is the more complex part; take your time to see how each horn leads to the same conclusion.
4
See how both aspects block the inference from miracle to truthfulness
In both cases, the miracle cannot serve as proof of the prophet's truthfulness. This objection challenges the very foundation of proving prophethood through miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثالث : سلمنا ان فاعلها هو الله تعالى ؛ فلم قلتم إنه تعالى فعلها لغرض التصديق ؟ وتقريره من وجهين :
الأول : أنكم أقمتم الدلائل القاهرة على أنه يمتنع أن تكون أفعال الله تعالى وأحكامه معللة بشيء من الأغراض والمقاصد، وإذا كان كذلك امتنع القول بأنه تعالى إنما خلق هذه المعجزات لأجل غرض التصديق.
الوجه الثاني : هو أنا نقول : الفعل إما أن يكون موقوفا على الداعي وإما أن لا يكون، فإن كان موقوفا على الداعي كان الجبر لازما ـ على ما قررناه في مسألة خلق الأفعال - وإذا لزم الجبر كان الباري تعالى هو الخالق للكفر والمعاصي؛ لأنه هو الذي فعل القدرة والداعية اللتين مجموعهما يوجب هذه القبائح وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن الإضلال ممتنعا في حق الله تعالى، وإذا كان كذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى أظهر هذه المعجزات على يد هذا الكاذب لأجل الإضلال؟ وأما إذا قلنا إن الفعل غير موقوف على الداعي فحينئذ لا يبعد أن يقال : أنه تعالى خلق هذه المعجزة لا لغرض البتة، وحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بحق المعجزة على التصديق.
The third objection: Granting that the agent of [the miracles] is God Almighty, why did you say that He, exalted, did them for the purpose of confirmation (tasdeeq)? Its exposition is from two aspects: First: You have established conclusive proofs that it is impossible for God's actions and judgments to be motivated by any purposes and objectives. If so, one cannot say that He only created these miracles for the purpose of confirmation. The second aspect: We say: Either an act is dependent on a motive (da'ii) or not. If it is dependent on a motive, then determinism (jabr) becomes necessary – as we established in the issue of the creation of actions. If determinism is necessary, then the Creator is the creator of disbelief and sins, because He is the one who created the power and the motive which together necessitate these evil acts. If that is the case, then misguidance is not impossible on the part of God. If so, why is it not permissible to say that He displayed these miracles at the hands of this liar for the purpose of misguidance? And if we say that the act is not dependent on a motive, then it is not improbable to say that He created this miracle for no purpose at all, and then it is impossible to infer confirmation from the miracle.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents a third major objection against using miracles as proof of prophethood. It assumes the miracles are from God but challenges the inference that they are for tasdeeq (confirming truthfulness). The objection has two aspects. The first appeals to a theological principle: God's actions are not motivated by purposes. So claiming that God performed miracles for the purpose of confirmation contradicts that principle. The second aspect is more intricate. It presents a dilemma: either actions depend on a motive (da'ii) or they don't. If they depend on a motive, then determinism follows (since the motive and power together cause the act, making God the creator of all acts, including evil). If God creates both the power and the motive for evil acts, then misguidance is possible, and a miracle could be performed to mislead. If actions do not depend on a motive, then a miracle could be performed for no purpose at all, so it cannot be used as evidence of truthfulness. Either way, the inference from miracle to confirmation is invalid.

Key Terms
التصديق
الجبر
خلق الأفعال
الداعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miracle always indicates divine approval.
Correction: This objection shows that a miracle could be for misguidance or purposeless, thus not necessarily indicating divine approval.

Checkpoint
According to the second aspect of the third objection, if an act depends on a motive (da'ii), what follows?
A) Free will is established.
B) God is the creator of both the motive and the capacity, leading to determinism.
C) Miracles cannot occur.
D) The act is meaningless.

Key Takeaway
The third objection undermines the evidential value of miracles by arguing they could be purposeless or even meant to mislead.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider a time when you assumed something good happening was a sign of divine approval. How does Razi's objection challenge that assumption? Are you willing to examine your own experiences with the same critical lens?
?
This objection shows that even the clearest evidence can be ambiguous if God wills it. What in your life do you treat as undeniable proof of truth? How would you know if that proof were actually leading you astray? What would you do about it?

The Fourth Objection: Exclusivity of Purpose
Imam al-Razi presents the fourth objection questioning why miracles must have only the purpose of confirming the prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the miracle argument, which questions the exclusivity of the purpose of miracles.

Core Idea
The fourth objection grants divine purposes but challenges the exclusivity of miracles for confirming prophets.

Imam al-Razi presents the fourth objection, which takes a different approach from the previous ones. It begins by conceding that God's actions are indeed based on purposes, but then shifts the question: why must the purpose of miracles be exclusively to confirm the claimant? This objection places the burden of proof back on the defender of the miracle argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: concession, challenge, demand for evidence.
The passage is short but packs a logical punch.
2
Identify what is granted and what is challenged
What does the objection grant? (God's actions have purposes.) What does it challenge? (That the only purpose of miracles is to confirm the claimant.)
3
Consider the logical strategy
Reflect on why this objection is effective: it does not deny divine purposes, but questions the specific claim of exclusivity, shifting the burden of proof.
This is a common dialectical move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الرابع سلمنا أن أفعال الله تعالى معللة برعاية الأغراض والمقاصد، إلا أنكم لم قلتم : إنه لا غرض لله تعالى ولا مقصود له من خلق المعجزات إلا تصديق هذا المدعي ؟ وما الدليل على هذا الحصر ؟
The fourth objection: We grant that God's actions are based on purposes and aims, but why do you say that there is no purpose or aim for God in creating miracles except confirming this claimant? And what is the evidence for this exclusivity?
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection, which takes a different approach from the third. It concedes that God's actions are indeed based on purposes and aims (contra the first aspect of the third objection). However, it challenges the specific claim that the only purpose of miracles is to confirm the truthfulness of the claimant. The objection asks for evidence that the purpose is exclusively confirmation, rather than some other divine purpose. This shifts the burden of proof onto the proponent of the miracle argument.

Key Terms

سلمنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this objection denies that God's actions have purposes altogether.
Correction: The objection explicitly grants that God's actions are based on purposes; it only questions the exclusivity of the purpose of miracles.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth objection grant?
A) That God's actions have no purposes.
B) That miracles confirm prophets.
C) That God's actions are based on purposes.
D) That miracles are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection forces the defender of the miracle argument to prove that the only purpose of miracles is to confirm the prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a religious claim, do you demand evidence for its exclusivity, or do you often assume it's the only explanation? How does this objection challenge your own tendency to accept exclusive claims without proof?
?

?
This objection teaches us to question assumptions of exclusivity. Where in your life have you assumed that your path is the only correct one, and how can you humbly examine that assumption today?

First Objection: Miracle as Beginning of a New Custom
Al-Razi presents the first voluntary objection: a miracle may be the beginning of a new natural custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that what appears as a miracle could be the initiation of a new natural custom rather than a supernatural event.

Core Idea
A miracle might just be the first instance of a new habit of nature.

Imam al-Razi begins his voluntary objections by considering a logical possibility: every natural custom must have a first occurrence. Could a miracle simply be that first occurrence, later becoming routine? This objection forces us to question whether rarity alone makes an event supernatural.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection, then its translation. Notice how al-Razi draws an analogy to the origination of the world.
The argument uses the principle that every custom has a beginning.
2
Identify the core analogy
The objection says: since the world began with an uncustomary event (creation), and then became customary, why can't the same apply to the claimed miracle?
3
Reflect on the implication
If a miracle is just the start of a new custom, then it does not necessarily confirm prophethood. Ask yourself: does this make the miracle ambiguous?
This objection challenges the certainty of miracles as proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نذكر وجوها أخر على سبيل التبرع : الأول : أن جملة هذه الأفعال الموافقة للعادات لا بد أن يكون لها أول لما ثبت أن العالم محدث، وحدوث كل واحد منها في المرة الأولى يكون على خلاف العادة، ثم إنه تعالى جعل ذلك دائمًا مستمراً فيما بعد ذلك، فكذا ههنا لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه سبحانه وتعالى أراد أن يبدأ بإحداث هذا الفعل الخارق للعادة ثم إنه يديمه بعد ذلك ويجعله عادة مستمرة بعد ذلك؟ وبالجملة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هذا الذي حدث هو ابتداء عادة وستصير مستمرة بعد ذلك ؟ الثاني : أنه يجوز أن يكون حدوث هذا الشيء على سبيل العادة إلا أنه عادة لا تحصل إلا في أزمنة متطاولة، وذلك لأن دور الفلك الثامن لا يتم - عند بعضهم - إلا في مدة سنة وثلاثين ألف سنة، وعلى هذا تكون العادة المستمرة المطردة هو أن كل كوكب يحصل في كل ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة في موضع من الفلك، فمن نظر إلى حصوله في ذلك الوقت ظن أنه خارق للعادة ومن عرف الوضع المقتضي لذلك علم أنه عادة متطاولة، فكذا ههنا يجوز أن يقال : هذا الذي حدث إنما حدث على وفق عادة متطاولة. الثالث : أنه لما انشق القمر على السماء فلعل إنسانا أخر في جانب آخر من الأرض ادعى النبوة وطلب من الله تعالى إظهار المعجزة في هذا الوقت، وهو تعالى إنما أظهره في ذلك الوقت تصديقا لذلك الإنسان لا لهذا الإنسان. الرابع : أن هذا الإنسان لما ادعى الرسالة على سبيل الكذب، وطلب من الله تعالى إظهار المعجزة فإن الله يظهر المعجزة على وفق دعواه حتى يصير موهما لكونه صادقًا، إلا انه لما قرر في عقل المكلف أنه يجوز أن يكون غرض الله تعالى منه شيئًا آخر سوى التصديق وكان خلق هذا المعجز يجري مجرى إنزال المتشابهات، وخلق الأوهام المورثة للشبهات، وكان المقصود منها أنه الاحتراز عنها، فإذا احترز المكلف عنها مع صعوبة الاحتراز عنها، كان ثوابه أعظم. وعلى الجملة فمن الذي يمكنه أن يعرف أقسام حكم الله تعالى في خلق الأشياء ؟ وحينئذ كان الجزم بأنه لا غرض لله تعالى في هذا الفعل إلا هذا الوجه الواحد تحكما من غير دليل، والله الهادي.
Then we mention additional aspects as a voluntary contribution: First: that the totality of these customary acts must have a beginning, since it is established that the world is originated, and the occurrence of each of them the first time is contrary to custom, then God made that continuous and perpetual thereafter. So here too, why is it not permissible to say that God desired to begin creating this extraordinary act and then perpetuate it and make it a continuous custom thereafter? In summary, why is it not permissible that this occurrence is the beginning of a custom that will become continuous afterwards? Second: that it is permissible that this thing occurs in a customary way, except that it is a custom that only occurs in long time intervals. Because the cycle of the eighth sphere is not completed—according to some—except in a period of 36,000 years. On this basis, the continuous, regular custom is that every planet occurs every 36,000 years in a position of the sphere. So whoever looks at its occurrence at that time thinks it is supernatural, while whoever knows the position that necessitates it knows that it is a long-standing custom. So here too, it is permissible to say: this occurrence happened according to a long-standing custom. Third: that when the moon split in the sky, perhaps another person in another side of the earth claimed prophethood and asked God to manifest a miracle at that time, and God manifested it at that time in confirmation of that person, not this person. Fourth: that when this person claimed prophethood falsely and asked God to manifest the miracle, God manifests the miracle in accordance with his claim so that it becomes suggestive of his truthfulness, except that it is established in the mind of the accountable that it is permissible that God's purpose from it is something other than confirmation. And the creation of this miracle is like sending down ambiguous verses and creating doubts that generate suspicions. The purpose of them is to be cautious about them. So when the accountable is cautious about them despite the difficulty of being cautious, his reward is greater. In summary, who can know the divisions of God's wisdom in creating things? At that point, asserting that there is no purpose for God in this act except this one aspect is arbitrary without evidence. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents four additional voluntary objections that provide alternative explanations for miracles, challenging the conclusion that miracles necessarily confirm a prophet. The first objection suggests that what appears as a miracle might be the beginning of a new natural custom, not a supernatural event. The second objection uses the example of extremely rare astronomical events (like planetary alignments every 36,000 years) to argue that something can be natural but appear miraculous due to long intervals. The third objection proposes that a miracle might be a response to a different prophet at the same time in another location, so it does not confirm the present claimant. The fourth objection is more theological: God may create miracles even at the hand of a liar to test people, making it harder for them to discern truth from falsehood, thus increasing their reward if they avoid being deceived. The passage concludes that it is arbitrary to claim that the only purpose of a miracle is confirmation, and only God knows His wisdom.

Key Terms
العادة
حدوث
ابتداء عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what is the analogy used to question the miraculous nature of an event?
A) The creation of the world
B) The splitting of the moon
C) The cycle of the eighth sphere
D) The sending of ambiguous verses

Key Takeaway
The first objection suggests that a miracle could be the first instance of a new natural custom, thus not necessarily a divine confirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you experienced an extraordinary event today, would you immediately attribute it to divine intervention, or would you consider that it might be the first instance of a new natural pattern? What does your answer reveal about the strength of your faith?
?
Al-Razi points out that even the first occurrence of a custom is contrary to that custom. Think of a time in your life when you encountered something unexpected. Did you seek to understand it as part of God's greater plan, or did you dismiss it as mere chance? How can this lesson train you to approach the unexpected with more humility and trust in God's wisdom?
?
The objection in this lesson could lead some to doubt the certainty of miracles. In what areas of your own belief are you most vulnerable to such skepticism? What steps will you take today to ground your faith in unshakable evidence, beyond just the extraordinary?

Second Objection: Miracle as a Rare Natural Event
Al-Razi presents the second objection: a miracle might be a natural event that occurs only after a very long period.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection uses extremely rare astronomical cycles to argue that apparent miracles may be natural events occurring at long intervals.

Core Idea
An event can be natural yet seem miraculous because it happens once in 36,000 years.

The second objection draws on the fact that some natural events, like planetary alignments, occur only after immense periods of time. To an observer unaware of the cycle, such an event appears miraculous. Al-Razi extends this logic to any rare occurrence, challenging the assumption that rarity implies supernatural intervention.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the specific example of the eighth sphere's cycle taking 36,000 years.
This is an astronomical reference common in classical Islamic cosmology.
2
Understand the rarity argument
The objection says that a custom can be continuous but only manifest after long intervals. Just because an event is rare does not mean it is uncustomary.
This challenges the definition of 'customary' as frequent.
3
Apply to miracles
If a claimed miracle is something that occurs extremely rarely, it might still be part of a natural cycle. How does this affect its value as a proof of prophethood?

Third Objection: Miracle Might Be for Another Prophet
Al-Razi presents the third objection: a miracle might be a response to a different prophetic claimant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that a miracle occurring in response to a claimant's request could actually be a confirmation of another, unseen claimant in a different location.

Core Idea
A miracle might confirm a different prophet in a distant place, not the one who claims it.

The third objection introduces a simple but powerful possibility: when a miracle occurs, it could be responding to the prayer of a different person who claimed prophethood elsewhere at the exact same time. Since we cannot rule out that possibility, the miracle does not definitively confirm the visible claimant.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The example given is the splitting of the moon, a well-known miracle attributed to Prophet Muhammad.
This objection uses a real miracle as a hypothetical scenario.
2
Understand the simultaneity problem
The objection says: perhaps another person in a different part of the world also prayed for a miracle at that moment. The split moon could be a response to him, not to the claimant we are considering.
This exploits the fact that we cannot know all simultaneous events.
3
Reflect on the implication
If a miracle can be ambiguous in its recipient, then it cannot serve as a conclusive proof for a specific prophet. How does this objection affect the argument from miracles?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نذكر وجوها أخر على سبيل التبرع : الأول : أن جملة هذه الأفعال الموافقة للعادات لا بد أن يكون لها أول لما ثبت أن العالم محدث، وحدوث كل واحد منها في المرة الأولى يكون على خلاف العادة، ثم إنه تعالى جعل ذلك دائمًا مستمراً فيما بعد ذلك، فكذا ههنا لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه سبحانه وتعالى أراد أن يبدأ بإحداث هذا الفعل الخارق للعادة ثم إنه يديمه بعد ذلك ويجعله عادة مستمرة بعد ذلك؟ وبالجملة فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هذا الذي حدث هو ابتداء عادة وستصير مستمرة بعد ذلك ؟ الثاني : أنه يجوز أن يكون حدوث هذا الشيء على سبيل العادة إلا أنه عادة لا تحصل إلا في أزمنة متطاولة، وذلك لأن دور الفلك الثامن لا يتم - عند بعضهم - إلا في مدة سنة وثلاثين ألف سنة، وعلى هذا تكون العادة المستمرة المطردة هو أن كل كوكب يحصل في كل ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة في موضع من الفلك، فمن نظر إلى حصوله في ذلك الوقت ظن أنه خارق للعادة ومن عرف الوضع المقتضي لذلك علم أنه عادة متطاولة، فكذا ههنا يجوز أن يقال : هذا الذي حدث إنما حدث على وفق عادة متطاولة. الثالث : أنه لما انشق القمر على السماء فلعل إنسانا أخر في جانب آخر من الأرض ادعى النبوة وطلب من الله تعالى إظهار المعجزة في هذا الوقت، وهو تعالى إنما أظهره في ذلك الوقت تصديقا لذلك الإنسان لا لهذا الإنسان. الرابع : أن هذا الإنسان لما ادعى الرسالة على سبيل الكذب، وطلب من الله تعالى إظهار المعجزة فإن الله يظهر المعجزة على وفق دعواه حتى يصير موهما لكونه صادقًا، إلا انه لما قرر في عقل المكلف أنه يجوز أن يكون غرض الله تعالى منه شيئًا آخر سوى التصديق وكان خلق هذا المعجز يجري مجرى إنزال المتشابهات، وخلق الأوهام المورثة للشبهات، وكان المقصود منها أنه الاحتراز عنها، فإذا احترز المكلف عنها مع صعوبة الاحتراز عنها، كان ثوابه أعظم. وعلى الجملة فمن الذي يمكنه أن يعرف أقسام حكم الله تعالى في خلق الأشياء ؟ وحينئذ كان الجزم بأنه لا غرض لله تعالى في هذا الفعل إلا هذا الوجه الواحد تحكما من غير دليل، والله الهادي.
Then we mention additional aspects as a voluntary contribution: First: that the totality of these customary acts must have a beginning, since it is established that the world is originated, and the occurrence of each of them the first time is contrary to custom, then God made that continuous and perpetual thereafter. So here too, why is it not permissible to say that God desired to begin creating this extraordinary act and then perpetuate it and make it a continuous custom thereafter? In summary, why is it not permissible that this occurrence is the beginning of a custom that will become continuous afterwards? Second: that it is permissible that this thing occurs in a customary way, except that it is a custom that only occurs in long time intervals. Because the cycle of the eighth sphere is not completed—according to some—except in a period of 36,000 years. On this basis, the continuous, regular custom is that every planet occurs every 36,000 years in a position of the sphere. So whoever looks at its occurrence at that time thinks it is supernatural, while whoever knows the position that necessitates it knows that it is a long-standing custom. So here too, it is permissible to say: this occurrence happened according to a long-standing custom. Third: that when the moon split in the sky, perhaps another person in another side of the earth claimed prophethood and asked God to manifest a miracle at that time, and God manifested it at that time in confirmation of that person, not this person. Fourth: that when this person claimed prophethood falsely and asked God to manifest the miracle, God manifests the miracle in accordance with his claim so that it becomes suggestive of his truthfulness, except that it is established in the mind of the accountable that it is permissible that God's purpose from it is something other than confirmation. And the creation of this miracle is like sending down ambiguous verses and creating doubts that generate suspicions. The purpose of them is to be cautious about them. So when the accountable is cautious about them despite the difficulty of being cautious, his reward is greater. In summary, who can know the divisions of God's wisdom in creating things? At that point, asserting that there is no purpose for God in this act except this one aspect is arbitrary without evidence. And God is the Guide.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents four additional voluntary objections that provide alternative explanations for miracles, challenging the conclusion that miracles necessarily confirm a prophet. The first objection suggests that what appears as a miracle might be the beginning of a new natural custom, not a supernatural event. The second objection uses the example of extremely rare astronomical events (like planetary alignments every 36,000 years) to argue that something can be natural but appear miraculous due to long intervals. The third objection proposes that a miracle might be a response to a different prophet at the same time in another location, so it does not confirm the present claimant. The fourth objection is more theological: God may create miracles even at the hand of a liar to test people, making it harder for them to discern truth from falsehood, thus increasing their reward if they avoid being deceived. The passage concludes that it is arbitrary to claim that the only purpose of a miracle is confirmation, and only God knows His wisdom.

Key Terms
انشقاق القمر
ادعى النبوة
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What specific miracle does the third objection use as an example?
A) The creation of the world
B) The splitting of the moon
C) The cycle of the eighth sphere
D) The sending of ambiguous verses

Key Takeaway
The third objection shows that a miracle might confirm a different prophet in another location, so it cannot be taken as definitive proof for the visible claimant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something you consider a miracle or sign in your own life, do you automatically assume it confirms your beliefs? How can you be certain that it isn't a sign for someone else or even a test? This objection forces you to question the exclusivity of divine signs—what does that mean for your personal reliance on such experiences?
?
Have you ever dismissed a logical objection like Al-Razi's as merely 'hypothetical' without honestly confronting its challenge? What would it cost you to admit that your evidence for a belief might be ambiguous? Are you willing to examine the standard of proof you require for truth claims, even uncomfortable ones?
?
If a miracle could theoretically confirm a false prophet, how does that change the weight you place on extraordinary events as a basis for faith? What other criteria must you use to be certain? This lesson demands that you look beyond the spectacle and ask: what else must be true for this sign to be reliable?

Fourth Objection: Miracle as a Divine Test
Al-Razi presents the fourth objection: God may create miracles for reasons other than confirmation, such as testing believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that God may create miracles at the hands of liars to test people, increasing the reward of those who remain cautious.

Core Idea
Miracles can be tests: God allows ambiguity to reward those who exercise caution.

The fourth objection goes deeper into divine wisdom: God might deliberately create miracles in support of a false prophet to test people's discernment. Just as the Quran contains ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) to test believers, miracles can be ambiguous signs. Those who overcome the difficulty of not being deceived are rewarded more greatly.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the comparison to ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) and the concept of reward for caution.
This objection is the most theological, referencing divine wisdom.
2
Understand the test rationale
God's purpose may be to create confusion so that the accountable person must exert effort to avoid deception. The greater the difficulty, the greater the reward.
3
Grasp the concluding point
The passage ends by stating that it is arbitrary to claim that the only purpose of a miracle is confirmation. Only God knows His wisdom.
