The First Station: Co-occurrence Does Not Prove Causality
The deniers challenge co-occurrence implying causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the deniers argue that the co-occurrence of a miracle and a prophetic claim does not necessarily prove causality.

Core Idea
Co-occurrence does not equal causality; other explanations are always possible.

Razi presents the first of two objections from the deniers. They challenge the assumption that when a miracle occurs upon a claim, the miracle is caused by the claim. The deniers argue that other causes could be present, so certainty is not attained.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the deniers' argument.
Notice the use of 'قلم' (pen) and 'قلنسوة' (cap) as examples.
2
Identify the deniers' claim about co-occurrence
The deniers concede that the act happened at the time of the request (co-occurrence), but they deny that this proves causality.
They introduce the idea of 'دوران وجودا وعدما' (co-occurrence in existence and non-existence).
3
Understand the counterexamples
The deniers give alternative explanations: a scorpion, a headache, or a gesture from another person. These show that another cause could have prompted the act.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The deniers conclude that while the intended meaning (that the king supports the claimant) may be apparent, it is not certain.
They say 'أما القطع فلا سبيل إليه' (as for certainty, there is no way to it).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال المنكرون وأما ما عولتم عليه من الدلائل في بيان أن المعجزات تدل على الصدق فحاصل الكلام فيه أنكم ادعيتم أن في الشاهد إقدام الملك على الفعل الخارق للعادة يدل على كونه مصدقا للمدعى في دعواه، وإذا ثبت ذلك في الشاهد فحينئذ تقيسون الغائب عليه فلنبحث عن كل واحد من هذين المقامين:
أما المقام الأول: فنقول: لا نسلم أن ظهور ذلك الفعل من الملك، يدل على أنه يصدق المدعي في دعواه ولا ننكر أنه يوهم ذلك، وأما أن يدل عليه قطعا فهذا ممنوع، وذلك لأن أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إنه حصل ذلك الفعل عند ذلك الطلب، ولم يفعله قبل ذلك الطلب فحصل الدوران بينهما وجودا وعدما، والدوران وجودا وعدما يدل على العلية.
إلا أنا نقول قد بينا أن الدوران وجودا وعدمًا لا يدل على العلية والذي يحقق ذلك أنه لا يبعد أن يقال: لما التمس ذلك المدعي من ذلك الملك أن يحرك قلنسوته، فإنه حصل في ذلك الوقت سبب آخر، واستقل بأن يحرك الملك قلنسوته لأجله، لا لأجل طلب المدعي، إما لأجل أن عقربا وقع على قلنسوته في ذلك الوقت، أو لأجل انه تألم رأسه من تلك القلنسوة في ذلك الوقت، أو لأجل أن إنسانًا آخر أشار إليه إشارة اقتضت أن يحرك الملك قلنسوته وبالجملة فالمعلوم أنه لا بد للملك من غرض في تحريك القلنسوة، فأما انه لا غرض إلا تصديق هذا المدعي، فهذا لا شك أنه ظاهر، وأما القطع فلا سبيل إليه.
المقام الثاني: هب أنه ثبت هذا المعنى في الشاهد فكيف يقيسون الغائب عليه؟ وطريق السؤال عليه من وجهين:
الأول: أن القياس لا يفيد اليقين لاحتمال أن ما لأجله وقعت المفارقة بين الأصل والفرع معتبر في الأصل أو مانع في الفرع.
الثاني: أنا عرفنا بأحوال ذلك الملك وبأخلاقه ومناهج أفعاله، فلا جرم يمكننا أن تعرف أنه إنما فعل ذلك الفعل لأجل ذلك الغرض، وأما أنواع حكم الله تعالى في أفعاله ومخلوقاته، فليس لأحد سبيل إلى معرفتها ولا قدرة لأحد على الاطلاع عليها، ولهذا قال: ما أشهدتهم خلق السموات والأرض) [الكهف: ٥١] وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فكيف يمكن قياس أفعال الله تعالى على أفعال العباد؟
هذا جملة الكلام في السؤال، وبالله التوفيق.
Then the deniers said: As for what you rely on from the evidences in explaining that miracles indicate truthfulness, the gist of the speech therein is that you claimed that in the witnessed realm, the king's undertaking of a superhabitual act indicates that he is supporting the claimant in his claim, and if that is established in the witnessed, then you analogize the absent to it. So let us examine each of these two stations:
As for the first station: We say: We do not concede that the occurrence of that act from the king indicates that he supports the claimant in his claim, nor do we deny that it suggests that. But that it indicates it definitively, this is prevented. That is because the utmost in this matter is to say: That act occurred at the time of that request, and he did not do it before that request, so there is co-occurrence between them in existence and non-existence, and co-occurrence in existence and non-existence indicates causality.
Except that we say: We have shown that co-occurrence in existence and non-existence does not indicate causality. What confirms that is that it is not improbable to say: When that claimant asked that king to move his cap, another cause occurred at that time and was independent in causing the king to move his cap for its sake, not for the sake of the claimant's request—either because a scorpion fell on his cap at that time, or because his head hurt from that cap at that time, or because another person gestured to him a gesture that necessitated that the king move his cap. In short, it is known that the king must have a purpose in moving the cap. As for there being no purpose except supporting this claimant, this is no doubt apparent, but as for certainty, there is no way to it.
The second station: Grant that this meaning is established in the witnessed, then how do they analogize the absent to it? The way of questioning it is from two aspects:
First: That analogy does not yield certainty due to the possibility that that for which the difference occurred between the original and the branch is either a condition in the original or an impediment in the branch.
Second: We know the states of that king, his morals, and the patterns of his actions, so inevitably we can know that he did that act for that purpose. But as for the types of God's wisdom in His actions and creatures, no one has a means to know them nor any ability to be informed of them. For this He said: 'I did not make them witness the creation of the heavens and the earth' [al-Kahf: 51]. If this is the case, then how can the actions of God be analogized to the actions of servants?
This is the totality of the speech in the questioning, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the deniers' objection against the argument that miracles (mu'jizat) indicate the truthfulness of a prophet. The deniers argue that the inference from a miracle to truthfulness is not certain. They structure their objection into two stations (maqam).

First station: They challenge the premise that in the witnessed realm (al-shahid), a king's extraordinary act at the request of a claimant proves the king's intent to support him. They concede it may suggest (yuhimmu) that, but not definitively. The reason is that the co-occurrence (al-dawaran) of the act with the request does not prove causality ('illiyya), because another cause could have prompted the act. They give examples: a scorpion falling on the king's cap, a headache, or another's gesture. Thus, the act could have any number of purposes, not necessarily supporting the claimant.

Second station: Even if (hubb) the meaning were established in the witnessed realm, how can one analogically transfer (qiyas) it to the absent realm (al-gha'ib), i.e., God? They give two reasons: First, analogy in general does not yield certainty because the factor causing the similarity may be absent or an impediment may exist. Second, in the case of a human king, we know his character and motives, but God's actions are beyond our knowledge—as the Quran says 'I did not make them witness the creation of the heavens and the earth.' Hence, we cannot know God's purposes or analogize from human actions to divine actions.

The deniers conclude this summary of their objection with 'This is the totality of the speech in the questioning, and with God is success.'

Key Terms
معجزة
دوران
علية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the deniers deny miracles altogether; they only deny the logical inference from miracles to truthfulness.
Correction: The deniers accept that miracles occur but argue that their co-occurrence with a claim does not ensure causality.

Checkpoint
According to the deniers, why does the co-occurrence of a request and an extraordinary act not prove the king's intent to support the claimant?
A) Because the king might have acted out of fear of the claimant.
B) Because another cause could have prompted the act, such as a scorpion.
C) Because the king cannot lie in the witnessed realm.
D) Because the act is not extraordinary enough.

Key Takeaway
Co-occurrence of a miracle and a claim does not guarantee a causal link; plausible alternative explanations undermine certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a seemingly miraculous answer to your prayer, do you automatically conclude that it was a direct response from God? What other factors—like your own readiness, the timing, or other hidden causes—might have played a role? How willing are you to consider those alternatives before claiming certainty?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely sure that event A caused event B because they happened together. What if you had a blind spot to another cause operating behind the scenes? How does this lesson challenge your confidence in your own causal reasoning, especially in matters of faith?
?
If the mere co-occurrence of a miracle and a claim is not enough for certainty, then what level of evidence would satisfy you to accept a prophet's truthfulness? Are you willing to live with probability rather than certainty? What does that mean for your faith?

The Second Station: The Problem of Analogy from Human Kings to God
The deniers criticize analogy from the witnessed to the absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogizing from human kings to God is problematic according to the deniers.

Core Idea
Analogy fails because we know human motives but not God's wisdom.

The second objection denies that even if the first station is granted, the analogy from witnessed to absent is valid. The deniers give two reasons: the uncertainty inherent in analogy and our ignorance of God's intentions, citing the Quranic verse 'I did not make them witness the creation of the heavens and the earth.'

Guided Path
1
Read the second station passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the two objections raised by the deniers.
2
Identify the first objection about analogy
The deniers state that analogy (qiyas) does not yield certainty because important differences between the original (king) and branch (God) may exist.
The phrase 'احتمال أن ما لأجله وقعت المفارقة' highlights this.
3
Identify the second objection about knowledge
We know the character of a human king, but we cannot know God's wisdom or patterns of action. The Quranic verse is cited as proof.
The verse 'ما أشهدتهم خلق السموات والأرض' is key.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The deniers conclude that analogizing God's actions to human actions is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال المنكرون وأما ما عولتم عليه من الدلائل في بيان أن المعجزات تدل على الصدق فحاصل الكلام فيه أنكم ادعيتم أن في الشاهد إقدام الملك على الفعل الخارق للعادة يدل على كونه مصدقا للمدعى في دعواه، وإذا ثبت ذلك في الشاهد فحينئذ تقيسون الغائب عليه فلنبحث عن كل واحد من هذين المقامين:
أما المقام الأول: فنقول: لا نسلم أن ظهور ذلك الفعل من الملك، يدل على أنه يصدق المدعي في دعواه ولا ننكر أنه يوهم ذلك، وأما أن يدل عليه قطعا فهذا ممنوع، وذلك لأن أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إنه حصل ذلك الفعل عند ذلك الطلب، ولم يفعله قبل ذلك الطلب فحصل الدوران بينهما وجودا وعدما، والدوران وجودا وعدما يدل على العلية.
إلا أنا نقول قد بينا أن الدوران وجودا وعدمًا لا يدل على العلية والذي يحقق ذلك أنه لا يبعد أن يقال: لما التمس ذلك المدعي من ذلك الملك أن يحرك قلنسوته، فإنه حصل في ذلك الوقت سبب آخر، واستقل بأن يحرك الملك قلنسوته لأجله، لا لأجل طلب المدعي، إما لأجل أن عقربا وقع على قلنسوته في ذلك الوقت، أو لأجل انه تألم رأسه من تلك القلنسوة في ذلك الوقت، أو لأجل أن إنسانًا آخر أشار إليه إشارة اقتضت أن يحرك الملك قلنسوته وبالجملة فالمعلوم أنه لا بد للملك من غرض في تحريك القلنسوة، فأما انه لا غرض إلا تصديق هذا المدعي، فهذا لا شك أنه ظاهر، وأما القطع فلا سبيل إليه.
المقام الثاني: هب أنه ثبت هذا المعنى في الشاهد فكيف يقيسون الغائب عليه؟ وطريق السؤال عليه من وجهين:
الأول: أن القياس لا يفيد اليقين لاحتمال أن ما لأجله وقعت المفارقة بين الأصل والفرع معتبر في الأصل أو مانع في الفرع.
الثاني: أنا عرفنا بأحوال ذلك الملك وبأخلاقه ومناهج أفعاله، فلا جرم يمكننا أن تعرف أنه إنما فعل ذلك الفعل لأجل ذلك الغرض، وأما أنواع حكم الله تعالى في أفعاله ومخلوقاته، فليس لأحد سبيل إلى معرفتها ولا قدرة لأحد على الاطلاع عليها، ولهذا قال: ما أشهدتهم خلق السموات والأرض) [الكهف: ٥١] وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فكيف يمكن قياس أفعال الله تعالى على أفعال العباد؟
هذا جملة الكلام في السؤال، وبالله التوفيق.
Then the deniers said: As for what you rely on from the evidences in explaining that miracles indicate truthfulness, the gist of the speech therein is that you claimed that in the witnessed realm, the king's undertaking of a superhabitual act indicates that he is supporting the claimant in his claim, and if that is established in the witnessed, then you analogize the absent to it. So let us examine each of these two stations:
As for the first station: We say: We do not concede that the occurrence of that act from the king indicates that he supports the claimant in his claim, nor do we deny that it suggests that. But that it indicates it definitively, this is prevented. That is because the utmost in this matter is to say: That act occurred at the time of that request, and he did not do it before that request, so there is co-occurrence between them in existence and non-existence, and co-occurrence in existence and non-existence indicates causality.
Except that we say: We have shown that co-occurrence in existence and non-existence does not indicate causality. What confirms that is that it is not improbable to say: When that claimant asked that king to move his cap, another cause occurred at that time and was independent in causing the king to move his cap for its sake, not for the sake of the claimant's request—either because a scorpion fell on his cap at that time, or because his head hurt from that cap at that time, or because another person gestured to him a gesture that necessitated that the king move his cap. In short, it is known that the king must have a purpose in moving the cap. As for there being no purpose except supporting this claimant, this is no doubt apparent, but as for certainty, there is no way to it.
The second station: Grant that this meaning is established in the witnessed, then how do they analogize the absent to it? The way of questioning it is from two aspects:
First: That analogy does not yield certainty due to the possibility that that for which the difference occurred between the original and the branch is either a condition in the original or an impediment in the branch.
Second: We know the states of that king, his morals, and the patterns of his actions, so inevitably we can know that he did that act for that purpose. But as for the types of God's wisdom in His actions and creatures, no one has a means to know them nor any ability to be informed of them. For this He said: 'I did not make them witness the creation of the heavens and the earth' [al-Kahf: 51]. If this is the case, then how can the actions of God be analogized to the actions of servants?
This is the totality of the speech in the questioning, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the deniers' objection against the argument that miracles (mu'jizat) indicate the truthfulness of a prophet. The deniers argue that the inference from a miracle to truthfulness is not certain. They structure their objection into two stations (maqam).

First station: They challenge the premise that in the witnessed realm (al-shahid), a king's extraordinary act at the request of a claimant proves the king's intent to support him. They concede it may suggest (yuhimmu) that, but not definitively. The reason is that the co-occurrence (al-dawaran) of the act with the request does not prove causality ('illiyya), because another cause could have prompted the act. They give examples: a scorpion falling on the king's cap, a headache, or another's gesture. Thus, the act could have any number of purposes, not necessarily supporting the claimant.

Second station: Even if (hubb) the meaning were established in the witnessed realm, how can one analogically transfer (qiyas) it to the absent realm (al-gha'ib), i.e., God? They give two reasons: First, analogy in general does not yield certainty because the factor causing the similarity may be absent or an impediment may exist. Second, in the case of a human king, we know his character and motives, but God's actions are beyond our knowledge—as the Quran says 'I did not make them witness the creation of the heavens and the earth.' Hence, we cannot know God's purposes or analogize from human actions to divine actions.

The deniers conclude this summary of their objection with 'This is the totality of the speech in the questioning, and with God is success.'

Key Terms
قياس
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a miracle occurs, it must be from God and thus proves prophethood. The deniers argue that even if the miracle is from God, we cannot know the purpose without analogical reasoning, which is uncertain.
Correction: The deniers do not deny that miracles are from God; they deny that we can infer the specific purpose (supporting a claimant) through analogy.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason the deniers give for rejecting analogy from human kings to God?
A) Human kings always act for the same reasons.
B) We know God's wisdom through revelation.
C) We do not have access to the wisdom behind God's actions.
D) The king's actions are always extraordinary.

Key Takeaway
Analogy from human kings to God fails because we know human motives but not God's wisdom, and analogy itself is uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one area of your life where you have been trying to understand God's actions by comparing them to human actions? How has the lesson's warning about analogy challenged that thinking? What will you do to submit to God's hidden wisdom instead?
?
The deniers say we cannot know God's wisdom in His creatures. How does that confront your desire to have everything in your life make sense to you? Will you trust God even when you cannot see the wisdom? Write a short prayer of surrender.
?
Reflect on a time when you confidently assumed you knew God's intention based on your own reasoning. In light of this passage, how might you have been wrong? What step will you take today to humble your intellect before God's hidden purposes?

First Response: Breaking Habits is Possible
Razi responds that breaking habits is possible via earlier proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that breaking habits is impossible by appealing to prior proofs about celestial bodies and the principle of uniformity among bodies.

Core Idea
What is possible for one body is possible for all, so miracles are not impossible.

In this passage, Razi directly counters the first objection of those who deny the possibility of miracles. He does not start from scratch; instead, he points back to already established proofs. This shows how kalam arguments build on prior foundations. As you read, notice how he uses a general principle to answer a specific objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic and English translation together. Notice how Razi quotes the denier's claim and then responds.
The denier's claim is embedded in the passage.
2
Identify the two parts of the response
Razi gives two reasons: (1) We already proved that opening and closing are possible for celestial bodies. (2) Whatever is possible for one body is possible for all bodies. Identify these in the text.
3
Reflect on the argument's structure
Ask yourself: Why does Razi refer to celestial bodies? How does the general principle strengthen his case? This shows a logical chain from prior proof to universal application.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله أولا: "انخراق العادات" محال قلنا: قد بينا أن الخرق والالتئام على الأجرام الفلكية جائزان، وأيضًا بينا أن ما يصح على جسم يصح على سائر الأجسام.
His saying, first: 'The breaking of habits is impossible.' We say: We have shown that opening and closing upon the celestial bodies are both possible, and also we have shown that whatever is possible for one body is possible for all bodies.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is the first response (jawab) by Imam Razi to an objection raised by the deniers. The deniers claimed that the breaking of habits (inkhiraq al-'adat) is impossible (muhal). Razi counters by referring to earlier proofs: he says they have already demonstrated that opening (al-kharq) and closing (al-ilti'am) upon the celestial bodies (al-ajram al-falakiyya) are both possible (ja'izan). Moreover, they have established that what is possible for one body is possible for all bodies (sā'ir al-ajsam). Thus, the occurrence of miracles—which involve breaking natural habits—is not impossible. This refutes the first objection.

Key Terms
انخراق العادات (breaking of habits)
الخرق والالتئام (opening and closing)
الأجرام الفلكية (celestial bodies)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'breaking habits' means violating the laws of nature entirely.
Correction: It only means a temporary suspension of the usual pattern, not a violation of divine order.

Checkpoint

A) He argues that breaking habits is a matter of faith, not reason.
B) He cites earlier proofs that celestial bodies can be opened and closed, and what holds for one body holds for all.
C) He says that breaking habits is only impossible for God, not for prophets.
D) He denies that miracles involve breaking habits.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first response to the denial of miracles is to invoke prior proof that celestial bodies can undergo change, then generalize to all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi argues that what is possible for one body is possible for all. How does this principle change the way you view seemingly impossible situations in your own life? Are there areas where you have limited God's power by assuming a pattern cannot be broken?
?
This passage refutes the denial of miracles. Do you live as if you expect miracles, or have you grown comfortable with a naturalistic worldview? Commit to one specific prayer request that you have been hesitant to ask because you thought it was 'impossible'.

Miracles Indicate Truthfulness by Convention
Razi explains that miracles indicate truthfulness by convention, not by God's purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the indicative power of miracles by analogy to conventional language, without appealing to divine purpose.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truthfulness the way words indicate meanings: by convention, not intention.

In this passage, Razi responds to a key objection: if God's actions are not motivated by purposes, how can miracles serve as signs of prophethood? He draws a powerful distinction between purpose and conventional indication. By comparing miracles to speech, he shows that supernatural events after a claim become indicators of divine support — not because God intended them as such, but by the very order He established.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Identify the opponent's claim: God's acts are not purposeful. Notice how Razi does not deny this, but shifts the ground.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فرق بين العلة المعروف'.
2
Understand the key distinction
Razi separates 'purpose' (gharad) from 'conventional indication' (dalala bi al-wad'). He denies that the miracle's creation was for the purpose of support, but affirms it makes support known.
This preserves divine transcendence while keeping miracles meaningful.
3
Analyze the analogy with speech
Just as words by convention indicate meanings in the speaker's mind, miracles by convention indicate God's support when they follow a claim.
The analogy is the heart of the response.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this refute the opponent? Even without purpose, miracles become signs by the very order God set, just as words become signs by human convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ثالثًا: "أن أفعال الله غير معللة بالأغراض" قلنا: فرق بين العلة المعروف ونحن لا ندعي أن خلق المعجز إنما كان لغرض التصديق، بل نقول: خلق المعجزة يعرف قيام التصديق بذات الله تعالى وكما أن هذه الكلمات المخصوصة صارت دالة بحسب الوضع والاصطلاح على المعانى القائمة بذات المتكلم، فكذلك هذه الأفعال الخارقة للعادة إذا حصلت عقيب الدعوى صارت دالة على قيام التصديق بذات من فعل المعجز.
His saying, third: 'That God's actions are not motivated by purposes.' We say: There is a difference between the known cause; and we do not claim that the creation of the miracle was for the purpose of supporting, rather we say: The creation of the miracle makes known the establishment of support in God's essence. Just as these specific words become indicative, by convention and usage, of the meanings subsisting in the speaker's essence, likewise these superhabitual actions, when they occur after the claim, become indicative of the establishment of support in the essence of the one who performs the miracle.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is the third response by Imam Razi. The deniers objected that God's actions are not motivated by purposes (gharad) and therefore miracles cannot be intended to support a claimant. Razi clarifies by distinguishing between a causal purpose (al-'illa al-ma'ruf) and a conventional indicator. He explicitly denies that they claim the creation of the miracle was for the purpose (gharad) of support (tasdiq). Instead, he argues that the creation of the miracle makes known (yu'arraf) the existence of support in God's essence (dhat Allah). He draws an analogy: just as particular words (al-kalimat al-makhsusa) become indicative (dalla) by convention (wad') and usage (istilah) of the meanings (al-ma'ani) that subsist in the speaker's essence (dhat al-mutakallim), similarly, these superhabitual actions (al-af'al al-khariqa lil-'ada), when they occur after the claim (du'a), become indicative (dalla) of the existence of support (qiyam al-tasdiq) in the essence of the one who performs the miracle (dhat man fa'ala al-mu'jiz). Thus, miracles indicate truthfulness directly by divine arrangement, not as a product of God's purpose.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
غرض (purpose)
وضع (convention)
اصطلاح (usage)
دلالة (indication)
تصديق (support/truthfulness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Razi denies all purpose in God's actions, or that the miracle's indication requires a separate act of will.
Correction: Razi denies only that the miracle's creation was for the purpose of support; he affirms that the miracle itself, by God's usual way, reveals support without being a purposeful act.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's analogy in this passage?
A) Miracles are like words because both require purpose.
B) Just as words indicate meanings by convention, miracles indicate support by convention.
C) Miracles are better than words because they are divine.
D) Razi rejects the idea that miracles indicate truthfulness.

Key Takeaway
Miracles indicate truthfulness by divine convention, just as words indicate meanings by human convention — no additional purpose is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see clear evidence of truth—like a miracle or a convincing proof—do you still resist because it doesn't come in the form you expected? How does Razi's point about convention challenge your demand that signs must fit your preferences? Will you commit today to accepting signs as God gives them, not as you imagine they should be?
?
Do you ever feel that your faith must be validated by your own understanding of purpose and intention? What if God's proof works by His arrangement, not by your approval? Examine your heart: are you willing to submit to a sign that is clear in itself, even if you cannot fully grasp the divine wisdom behind it?
?
Think of a time you refused to accept a truth because it seemed 'not enough' or 'not on your terms'. Could it be that you were demanding a purpose-driven sign while God had already given a convention-driven sign? Will you now learn to recognize signs by their intrinsic indication, and let that change how you respond to the call of truth starting today?

Moses' Miracle Shows Miracles Necessarily Indicate Truth
Razi uses a proof from Moses' miracle to show miracles exclusively indicate truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that miracles are specifically for indicating a prophet's truthfulness, using the example of Moses and the mountain.

Core Idea
Miracles force necessary knowledge of truth.

Imam Razi continues addressing objections that miracles could serve other purposes. Here he argues that miracles are determinate for supporting the prophet's claim. He illustrates this with the example of Prophet Moses, where the mountain's movement directly corresponded to the people's belief or disbelief, making the truth evident to all. This lesson examines that compelling proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Start by reading the first part where Razi quotes the objection that miracles might have other intentions. Then read his denial and claim of determinacy.
2
Examine the Moses example
Read the story where Moses asks God to move a mountain above his people, and it shifts based on their belief. Notice the cause-effect relationship.
3
Understand the necessary conclusion
Appreciate that Razi says everyone will necessarily know this indicates truth. Reflect on why this is necessary, not just probable.
This is key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله رابعا: "يحتمل أن يكون المقصود من خلق المعجزات أمورًا أخر سوى التصديق" قلنا: لا نسلم بل هو متعين للدلالة على حصول التصديق، والدليل عليه أن موسى لما قال: "إلهي إن كنت صادقا في ادعاء الرسالة فاجعل هذا الجبل في الهواء فوق رؤوسهم ثم إن القوم يشاهدون أنهم كلما آمنوا به تباعد الجبل عنهم، وكلما هموا بتكذيبه قرب أن يسقط عليهم، فعند هذا يعلم كل أحد بالضرورة هذا الأطلال تصديق المدعي في ادعاء الرسالة."
His saying, fourth: 'It is possible that the intent of creating miracles is other things besides support.' We say: We do not concede; rather it is determinate for indicating the occurrence of support. The proof of that is that when Moses said: 'My God, if I am truthful in claiming prophethood, then place this mountain in the air above their heads,' then the people witnessed that whenever they believed in him, the mountain moved away from them, and whenever they intended to deny him, it nearly fell upon them. At that point, everyone knows by necessity that this emergence [or manifestation] supports the claimant in his claim of prophethood.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is the fourth response by Imam Razi to the objection that miracles might have other purposes besides support. Razi argues that this is not possible; rather, the miracle is specifically (muta'ayyin) for indicating (dalal) the occurrence of support (husul al-tasdiq). He provides a proof (dalil): the example of Prophet Moses (Musa, peace be upon him). Moses asked God to move a mountain above the heads of his people as a sign: if they believed (amanu), the mountain would move away; if they intended to deny him (hammu bi-takdhibihi), it would nearly fall on them. Observing this behavior, everyone (kull ahad) would necessarily know (bi-al-darura) that this indicates the claimant (al-mudda'i) is truthful in claiming prophethood. The word 'al-atlal' may be a corruption, but the meaning is clear: the event is a manifest proof.

Key Terms
تعين (determination)
دلالة (indication)
تصديق (support)
ضرورة (necessity)
موسى (Moses)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the story is merely hypothetical and does not prove necessity.
Correction: Razi presents it as an actual event that yields necessary knowledge; the correlation between belief and the mountain's movement makes the conclusion inescapable.

Checkpoint
What does the example of Moses' people, where the mountain moves closer or farther based on their belief, illustrate?
A) That miracles are purely symbolic.
B) That the miracle is necessarily indicative of truthfulness.
C) That the people had free will to reject the miracle.
D) That the miracle served multiple purposes.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are designed to be unambiguous signs; the example of Moses shows that their very nature compels rational assent to the prophet's truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever witnessed a clear sign of Allah's truth in your life, yet found yourself hesitating or making excuses? What does that say about your heart?
?
The mountain moved when they believed, and nearly fell when they disbelieved. What 'mountain' has Allah placed before you that moves only when you submit? Are you still standing in defiance?
?
If miracles produce necessary knowledge, why do you sometimes feel uncertain about the truth of Islam? Is it a lack of evidence or a lack of surrender?

Miracles Provide Necessary Knowledge
Razi defends miracles as providing necessary knowledge against the objection of possible deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the concept of necessary knowledge to defend the argument from miracles against the objection that they might be deceptive.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge from experience trumps logical possibility.

Imagine seeing an old man and knowing with certainty he had parents, even though it's logically possible he didn't. Razi uses this everyday certainty to argue that miracles give us certain knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness, regardless of abstract doubts. Let's see how he builds this defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow: premise → example → application.
2
Identify the premise
Find where Razi says a thing can be possible in itself yet known by necessity not to occur. This is key to his argument.
3
Understand the example
Reflect on the old man example. Why does seeing an old man give necessary knowledge of his past? How does this relate to miracles?
It shows that customary experience creates certainty despite logical alternatives.
4
Apply to miracles
Notice how Razi applies the same logic: the miracle-indication relation is known by necessity, so the possibility of deception does not undermine it.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الجواب عن هذا السؤال : من انا نقدم على ذكر المقصود ،مقدمة، فنقول : إن الشيء قد يكون جائز الوقوع في ذلك فإنا نعلم علما ضروريًا أنه غير واقع ألا ترى أنا نجوز دخول ومع شخص في الوجود من غير أبوين، ونجوز أن يدخل في الوجود شيخ هرم من غير سبق الطفولية والشباب والكهولة، ثم إنا إذا أبصرنا شيخا علمنا بالضرورة أنه متولد من الأبوين، وأنه كان طفلا، ثم صار شاباً ثم صار شيخاً، وكذا القول في جميع الأمور العادية. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا قد بينا أن دلالة المعجزة على أن خلق المعجز لصدق المدعي معلوم بالضرورة كما ضربنا من المثال في إظلال الجبال، أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال عنه : يجوز أن تكون المعجزة مخلوقة لغرض آخر وهو الإضلال إلا أنا نقول : إن الشيء إذا علم وجوده بالضرورة لم يكن تجويز نقيضه يقدح في ذلك العلم الضروري كما بيناه في هذه المقدمة.
The second answer to this objection: We present a premise before mentioning the intended point. We say: A thing may be possible of occurrence in itself, yet we know with necessary knowledge that it is not actual. Do you not see that we allow the entrance [؟] of a person into existence without parents, and we allow a decrepit old man to come into existence without prior infancy, youth, and middle age. Then when we see an old man, we know by necessity that he is born from parents, and that he was a child, then became a youth, then became an old man. And likewise is the case with all customary matters. If you have understood this, then we say: We have already shown that the indication of the miracle that the Creator of the miracle intends to confirm the truthfulness of the claimant is known by necessity, as we gave the example of the overshadowing of mountains. The most that can be said about it is: It is possible that the miracle was created for another purpose, namely leading astray. But we say: When a thing is known to exist by necessity, the possibility of its contrary does not impair that necessary knowledge, as we have explained in this premise.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a second answer to an objection against the argument from miracles. Razi first establishes a crucial premise: a thing may be logically possible, yet we know with certainty (by necessity) that it does not happen. He gives the example of observing an old man: we know by necessity that he was born from parents and went through childhood, even though it is logically possible for someone to appear spontaneously as an old man. Similarly, the connection between a miracle and the truthfulness of the claimant is known by necessity from repeated experience. Even if one suggests that the miracle could be created for another purpose (like leading astray), that mere possibility does not undermine the necessary knowledge we have from witnessing the miracle. The argument does not rely on probability; it relies on certainty derived from direct experience.

Key Terms
معجزة (miracle)
علم ضروري (necessary knowledge)
جائز الوقوع (possible occurrence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mere possibility that a miracle could be deceptive undermines its argument for prophethood.
Correction: Razi shows that necessary knowledge is not shaken by abstract possibilities; just as we know an old man had parents despite logical alternatives, miracles give us certain knowledge of truthfulness.

Checkpoint
What example does Razi use to illustrate that something can be possible in itself yet known by necessity not to occur?
A) The overshadowing of mountains
B) The existence of an old man without parents
C) The creation of a miracle
D) The tying of a man's limbs

Key Takeaway
A miracle provides necessary knowledge of truthfulness because customary experience links them inseparably, just as we know an old man had parents.

First Objection of Deniers: Taklif Invalid Due to Determinism and Foreknowledge
Razi presents the deniers' first objection with two arguments for the invalidity of taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments used by deniers of prophethood to claim that religious obligation is impossible, one based on determinism and the other on divine foreknowledge.

Core Idea
Deniers argue prophethood is invalid because obligation requires freedom, which determinism and foreknowledge destroy.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the first objection raised by those who deny prophethood. They argue that the very concept of religious obligation (taklif) is impossible, and therefore sending prophets is futile. They support this with two distinct rational arguments: one from determinism (jabr) and one from divine foreknowledge. This lesson explores both arguments as presented by the deniers, setting the stage for Razi's subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall structure
The objection starts with a claim: taklif is invalid, so prophethood is invalid. Then two proofs are given for the invalidity of taklif. Identify the main premise and the two supporting arguments.
The passage is a presentation of the opposition's view, not Razi's own position.
2
Examine the first argument from determinism
Read the first proof carefully. It argues that because God creates all actions, humans have no real capability, so commanding them is like commanding a tied man thrown off a cliff to stand in the air – intuitively repugnant.
This argument uses the concept of badāhat al-‘aql (intuitive reason) to judge the repugnance of such a command.
3
Examine the second argument from foreknowledge
Read the second proof. It argues that God's foreknowledge makes obligation either futile (if obedience is known to occur) or unjust (if disobedience is known to occur). Notice the alternative formulation focusing on reward.
This argument relies on the logical implications of divine omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما منكرو النبوة فقد احتجوا بوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : قالوا : التكليف باطل، فالبعثة أيضا باطلة. أما المقدمة الأولى : - وهي أن التكليف باطل - فقد احتجوا عليه بوجوه : الأول : أن الجبر حق، فالتكليف باطل، وإنما قلنا : عن الجبر حق وذلك لأنا بينا في مسألة خلق الأفعال ان أفعال العباد مخلوقة لله تعالى وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فحال ما خلق الله تعالى تلك الأفعال لا يتمكن العبد من ترك الفعل، وحال ما لا يخلقها فيهم لا يتمكن من الفعل فثبت أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون العبد قادرًا البتة لا على الفعل ولا على الترك. وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كان الجبر حقا كان التكليف باطلا لأنا نعلم ببداهة العقل أن من قيد يدي إنسان ورجليه بالقيود الشديدة ثم ألقاه من شاهق جبل، ثم يقول له : قف في الهواء وإلا عذبتك عذابًا شديدًا مؤبداً ، فإن هذا مستقبح في بداهة العقل، ولو أنه كلف الأعمى بنقط المصاحف وكلف المفلوج أن يطير في الهواء، فجميع العقلاء يدركون قبح ذلك في بداهة عقولهم، ويقطعون بأن هذا لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين، فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الجبر حق، وثبت أنه متى كان الجبر حقا كان التكليف باطلًا. الثاني: أن الباري سبحانه وتعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات، والشيء الذي حصل التكليف به إما أن يكون معلوم الوقوع أو معلوم اللاوقوع، فإن كان معلوم الوقوع كان واجب الوقوع، فالتكليف به عبث وإن كان معلوم اللاوقوع كان الوقوع، فكان التكليف به ظلمًا فثبت أن القول بالتكليف باطل. ممتنع وربما ذكروا هذا الكلام في معرض آخر، وهو أن المقصود من التكليف جلب الثواب، وإن كان ذلك الثواب معلوم الوقوع فحينئذ لا حاجة البتة إلى فعل الطاعة، وإن كان معلوم اللاوقوع كان ممتنع الوقوع، وحينئذ لا فائدة في فعل الطاعة. الثالث: أن التكليف إما أن يتوجه حال استواء الداعي إلى الفعل والترك، أو
As for the deniers of prophethood, they have argued with [several] points: The first doubt: They said: Obligation (taklif) is invalid, so the sending of prophets (ba‘thah) is also invalid. As for the first premise — that taklif is invalid — they have argued for it with [several] points: First: That determinism (jabr) is true, so taklif is invalid. We say that jabr is true because we have shown in the issue of the creation of actions that the actions of servants are created by God Almighty. If this is the case, then when God creates those actions, the servant is unable to abandon the act, and when He does not create them in them, he is unable to do the act. So it is established that on this assumption the servant is in no way capable, neither of the act nor of abandonment. We say that when jabr is true, taklif is invalid, because we know by intuitive reason (badāhat al-‘aql) that if someone ties the hands and feet of a man tightly, then throws him from a high mountain, then says to him, "Stand in the air, otherwise I will punish you with severe everlasting punishment," this is deemed repugnant by intuitive reason. And if he were to oblige a blind man to copy the Quran and oblige a paralyzed man to fly in the air, all rational people perceive the repugnance of that by their intuitive reason and judge decisively that this is not befitting the Most Just of Judges. So it is established by what we have mentioned that jabr is true, and it is established that when jabr is true, taklif is invalid. Second: That the Creator, may He be exalted, knows all things. The thing that is the object of taklif is either known to occur or known not to occur. If it is known to occur, then it is necessary of occurrence, so taklif regarding it is futile. If it is known not to occur, then its occurrence is impossible, so taklif regarding it is unjust. Thus, the claim of taklif is invalid. And perhaps they mention this speech in another context: That the purpose of taklif is to bring about reward. If that reward is known to occur, then there is no need at all for performing obedience; if it is known not to occur, then its occurrence is impossible, and then there is no benefit in performing obedience. Third: That taklif is either directed when the motive to act and to refrain are equal, or...
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage begins a new section where the deniers of prophethood raise objections. The first objection is that prophethood is invalid because taklif (religious obligation) is invalid. They support this with three proofs. The first proof is from determinism (jabr): since God creates all actions, humans are not truly capable of choosing, so commanding them is like commanding a person tied and thrown off a cliff to stand in the air — intuitively repugnant. The second proof is from divine foreknowledge: God knows whether a person will obey or disobey. If He knows they will obey, then commanding is futile (since it will happen anyway); if He knows they will disobey, then commanding is unjust (since it is impossible for them to obey). They also give an alternative formulation focusing on reward. The third proof begins but is cut off; it concerns the state when the motive to act and not act are equal. The passage presents these as objections, not as Razi's own view.

Key Terms
تكليف (obligation)
جبر (determinism)
بداهة العقل (intuitive reason)
علم (foreknowledge)
بعثة (sending of prophets)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments are presented as true by Razi.
Correction: Razi is presenting the arguments of the deniers; he will refute them later.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if God knows that an act will not occur, what is the problem with obligating it?
A) It is futile
B) It is unjust
C) It is impossible
D) It is unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The deniers' first objection hinges on the claim that determinism and divine foreknowledge render religious obligation logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your sins are inevitable because God already knows them? This passage shows the deniers using such reasoning to reject divine commands. Does your heart ever whisper the same doubt? What is your response?
?
When you read the example of the bound man thrown from a cliff, did you feel intuitive repugnance? Now consider that you have obligations from God. Does your internal sense of justice ever question why God obliges you when He controls everything? How does this lesson help you confront that?
?
If someone were to convince you that God's foreknowledge makes your actions meaningless, would your daily prayers feel futile? How would you answer based on what you have learned from this passage about the deniers' reasoning?

The Preponderance of Motives Argument
Al-Razi presents the first of six arguments against obligation using motive theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against divine obligation based on the preponderance of motives.

Core Idea
If motives are equal, action is impossible; if one preponderates, action is necessary—so obligation is either impossible or redundant.

Imam al-Razi begins the first doubt by establishing a principle about motives. He argues that any action must either have a preponderant motive (making it necessary) or equal motives (making it impossible). Obligation then becomes either forcing what is already necessary or attempting the impossible. This sets the stage for the subsequent arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice how al-Razi presents two mutually exclusive cases.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage considers two possibilities: equality of motives and preponderance of one motive. State what al-Razi concludes about each.
Equality leads to impossibility, preponderance makes the act necessary.
3
Understand the implications for obligation
If obligation falls on the preponderant act, it commands what is already necessary; if on the inferior, it commands the impossible. Both are problematic.
This shows that the very concept of obligation may be incoherent.
4
Connect to broader themes
This argument relies on previous discussions on the creation of acts (خلق الأفعال). Consider how this fits into the wider debate on divine causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون
حال رجحان أحد الداعيين على الآخر، أما حال الاستواء فمحال؛ لأن الفعل ترجح، وحصول الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء جمع بين النقيضين، وأما حال الرجحان فمحال ؛ لأن الراجح واجب والمرجوح ممتنع - على ما بينا تقرير هذه المقدمة في مواضع - وإن وقع التكليف بالراجح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع شيء واجب الوقوع، وإن كان تكليفا بالمرجوح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع ممتنع الوقوع، وتمام هذا الوجه قد تقدم في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
تقرير
الرابع : أن التكليف لا فائدة فيه البتة، فكان عبئًا غير لائق بحكمة الحكيم. وإنما قلنا إنه لا فائدة فيه؛ لأن تلك الفائدة إن عادت إلى المعبود لزم أن يكون في محل النفع والضرر والزيادة والنقصان وهو محال وإن عادت إلى العابد فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن جميع الفوائد محصورة في أمرين : إما اللذة وإما السرور، أو ما يكون مفضيًا إليهما، وإما دفع الألم أو دفع الغم، أو ما يكون مفضيا إليهما، والمعبود تعالى قادر على تحصيل كل ذلك للعبد بدون واسطة التكليف، فكان توسط التكليف عبثا، وإذا ثبت هذا فظاهر أن العبث لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين.
الخامس: أن تكليف من علم أنه يكفر أو يفسق غير لائق بالحكمة؛ لأن المكلف به إذا دخل في الوجود لزم تجهيل المعبود، وإن لم يدخل في الوجود لزم استحقاق العبد للعقاب، وفعل شيء لا فائدة فيه - إلا أحد هذين الأمرين المحذورين - لا يليق بالحكمة.
السادس : أن التكليف يقتضي شغل القلب بتلك الأعمال، واشتغال القلب بغير يمنعه عن الاستغراق في معرفة الله ومحبته، وكل ما كان مانعا من معرفة الله ومحبته كان تركه من أوجب الواجبات.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الستة أن القول بالتكليف باطل، وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بالبعثة أيضًا باطلا؛ لأنه لا فائدة من البعثة إلا توجيه التكاليف على الخلق، وإذا كان المقصود باطلا كان التبع أولى بالبطلان.
The Thirtieth Issue: The case of preponderance of one of the two motives over the other. As for the case of equality, it is impossible because the action would be preponderant, and obtaining preponderance while equality obtains is a conjunction of contradictories. As for the case of preponderance, it is impossible because the preponderant is necessary and the inferior is impossible—according to what we have established of this premise in other places. If obligation (taklif) falls upon the preponderant, it is an obligation to bring about something necessarily occurring; and if it falls upon the inferior, it is an obligation to bring about something impossible of occurrence. The completion of this point has preceded in the issue of the creation of acts. Summary: Fourth: Obligation has absolutely no benefit, so it is a burden unbefitting the wisdom of the Wise. We say it has no benefit because if that benefit redounds to the Worshipped, it would necessitate that He is subject to benefit and harm, increase and decrease—which is impossible. And if it redounds to the worshipper, it is also false, because all benefits are confined to two things: either pleasure and joy, or what leads to them, or the repulsion of pain or grief, or what leads to them. And the Worshipped, exalted, is able to achieve all of that for the servant without the intermediary of obligation, so the intermediation of obligation is futility. If this is established, it is clear that futility is not befitting the Most Wise of judges. Fifth: Obligating one whom He knows will disbelieve or act wickedly is unbefitting of wisdom, because if the obligated act comes into existence, it necessitates ignorance of the Worshipped; and if it does not come into existence, it necessitates the servant's deserving punishment. Doing something with no benefit except one of these two objectionable things is not befitting of wisdom. Sixth: Obligation necessitates occupying the heart with those acts, and occupying the heart with other than [God] prevents it from absorption in knowledge of God and love of Him. Anything that prevents knowledge of God and love of Him, its abandonment is among the most obligatory duties. Thus, by these six aspects it is established that the claim of obligation is false. If this is established, the claim of sending messengers is also false, because there is no benefit from sending messengers except directing obligations upon creation. If the purpose is false, the subordinate is more deserving of falsehood.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the first doubt of those who deny prophethood. It consists of six rational arguments against divine obligation (taklif). The passage begins with a discussion of the preponderance of motives: if a motive is preponderant, the act becomes necessary; if motives are equal, the act is impossible. Thus obligation is either impossible or unnecessary. Then three numbered arguments follow. The fourth argument claims obligation has no benefit because God can directly grant all pleasures and remove all pains without it, so obligation is futile. The fifth argument points out that obligating someone God knows will disbelieve or sin leads either to God being ignorant (if the act occurs) or to punishment (if it doesn't), both unbefitting wisdom. The sixth argument says obligation distracts the heart from knowledge and love of God, which is the most important duty. The conclusion is that since obligation is impossible, sending prophets (whose purpose is to convey obligation) is also impossible.

Key Terms
تكليف (taklif, obligation)
رجحان (preponderance)
داعٍ (motive)
عبث (futility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderance argument, what happens if motives are equal?
A) The act is preponderant
B) The act is impossible
C) The act is obligatory
D) The act is optional

Key Takeaway
The preponderance argument shows that if motives are equal, action is impossible; if one preponderates, action is necessary—so obligation is either incoherent or redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific obligation you struggle to fulfill, like praying on time or lowering your gaze. What motives pull you away? According to this argument, is your failure due to equal motives or a preponderant opposing motive? How does this logical framework expose the reality of your inner battle, and what does it demand of you to seek help from Allah in tipping the scales?
?
If you truly believed that every action you take is determined by the strongest motive, would that change the way you view your own sins? Would you still feel responsible, or would you feel trapped? How does this argument challenge you to examine whether you live as a victim of your desires or as a conscious agent who must actively cultivate preponderant motives for obedience?
?
Al-Razi presents this doubt as a problem to be resolved. What doubts about God's commands lurk in your own heart? Are you willing to confront them through study and supplication, or do you prefer to ignore them? What is one step you can take this week to strengthen a weak motive for drawing closer to Allah?

The Fourth Argument: No Benefit in Obligation
Al-Razi presents the fourth argument that obligation has no benefit and is futile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against divine obligation: that obligation has no benefit and is therefore futile.

Core Idea
God can grant all benefits directly, so obligation is unnecessary and futile.

Al-Razi's fourth argument focuses on the utility of obligation. If God is all-powerful, He can provide all pleasures and remove all pains without requiring any acts of worship. Thus, imposing obligations serves no purpose and is mere futility, which is unbecoming of the Wise God.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument
Locate the section beginning with 'تقرير الرابع' and read it alongside the translation. Note the structure: claim, two possibilities, then refutation.
The argument is short but layered.
2
Identify the claim and its defense
Al-Razi claims obligation has no benefit. He supports this by considering benefit to God (impossible) or benefit to the servant (possible but unnecessary).
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Al-Razi argues that all benefits for the servant reduce to pleasure/pain avoidance. God can provide these directly, so obligation is superfluous. This makes obligation a form of 'عبث' (futility).
This is a powerful objection that challenges the very purpose of religion.
4
Reflect on its placement in the doubt
This is the fourth of six arguments. Consider how it builds on the first by further undermining the wisdom of obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون
حال رجحان أحد الداعيين على الآخر، أما حال الاستواء فمحال؛ لأن الفعل ترجح، وحصول الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء جمع بين النقيضين، وأما حال الرجحان فمحال ؛ لأن الراجح واجب والمرجوح ممتنع - على ما بينا تقرير هذه المقدمة في مواضع - وإن وقع التكليف بالراجح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع شيء واجب الوقوع، وإن كان تكليفا بالمرجوح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع ممتنع الوقوع، وتمام هذا الوجه قد تقدم في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
تقرير
الرابع : أن التكليف لا فائدة فيه البتة، فكان عبئًا غير لائق بحكمة الحكيم. وإنما قلنا إنه لا فائدة فيه؛ لأن تلك الفائدة إن عادت إلى المعبود لزم أن يكون في محل النفع والضرر والزيادة والنقصان وهو محال وإن عادت إلى العابد فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن جميع الفوائد محصورة في أمرين : إما اللذة وإما السرور، أو ما يكون مفضيًا إليهما، وإما دفع الألم أو دفع الغم، أو ما يكون مفضيا إليهما، والمعبود تعالى قادر على تحصيل كل ذلك للعبد بدون واسطة التكليف، فكان توسط التكليف عبثا، وإذا ثبت هذا فظاهر أن العبث لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين.
الخامس: أن تكليف من علم أنه يكفر أو يفسق غير لائق بالحكمة؛ لأن المكلف به إذا دخل في الوجود لزم تجهيل المعبود، وإن لم يدخل في الوجود لزم استحقاق العبد للعقاب، وفعل شيء لا فائدة فيه - إلا أحد هذين الأمرين المحذورين - لا يليق بالحكمة.
السادس : أن التكليف يقتضي شغل القلب بتلك الأعمال، واشتغال القلب بغير يمنعه عن الاستغراق في معرفة الله ومحبته، وكل ما كان مانعا من معرفة الله ومحبته كان تركه من أوجب الواجبات.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الستة أن القول بالتكليف باطل، وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بالبعثة أيضًا باطلا؛ لأنه لا فائدة من البعثة إلا توجيه التكاليف على الخلق، وإذا كان المقصود باطلا كان التبع أولى بالبطلان.
The Thirtieth Issue: The case of preponderance of one of the two motives over the other. As for the case of equality, it is impossible because the action would be preponderant, and obtaining preponderance while equality obtains is a conjunction of contradictories. As for the case of preponderance, it is impossible because the preponderant is necessary and the inferior is impossible—according to what we have established of this premise in other places. If obligation (taklif) falls upon the preponderant, it is an obligation to bring about something necessarily occurring; and if it falls upon the inferior, it is an obligation to bring about something impossible of occurrence. The completion of this point has preceded in the issue of the creation of acts. Summary: Fourth: Obligation has absolutely no benefit, so it is a burden unbefitting the wisdom of the Wise. We say it has no benefit because if that benefit redounds to the Worshipped, it would necessitate that He is subject to benefit and harm, increase and decrease—which is impossible. And if it redounds to the worshipper, it is also false, because all benefits are confined to two things: either pleasure and joy, or what leads to them, or the repulsion of pain or grief, or what leads to them. And the Worshipped, exalted, is able to achieve all of that for the servant without the intermediary of obligation, so the intermediation of obligation is futility. If this is established, it is clear that futility is not befitting the Most Wise of judges. Fifth: Obligating one whom He knows will disbelieve or act wickedly is unbefitting of wisdom, because if the obligated act comes into existence, it necessitates ignorance of the Worshipped; and if it does not come into existence, it necessitates the servant's deserving punishment. Doing something with no benefit except one of these two objectionable things is not befitting of wisdom. Sixth: Obligation necessitates occupying the heart with those acts, and occupying the heart with other than [God] prevents it from absorption in knowledge of God and love of Him. Anything that prevents knowledge of God and love of Him, its abandonment is among the most obligatory duties. Thus, by these six aspects it is established that the claim of obligation is false. If this is established, the claim of sending messengers is also false, because there is no benefit from sending messengers except directing obligations upon creation. If the purpose is false, the subordinate is more deserving of falsehood.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the first doubt of those who deny prophethood. It consists of six rational arguments against divine obligation (taklif). The passage begins with a discussion of the preponderance of motives: if a motive is preponderant, the act becomes necessary; if motives are equal, the act is impossible. Thus obligation is either impossible or unnecessary. Then three numbered arguments follow. The fourth argument claims obligation has no benefit because God can directly grant all pleasures and remove all pains without it, so obligation is futile. The fifth argument points out that obligating someone God knows will disbelieve or sin leads either to God being ignorant (if the act occurs) or to punishment (if it doesn't), both unbefitting wisdom. The sixth argument says obligation distracts the heart from knowledge and love of God, which is the most important duty. The conclusion is that since obligation is impossible, sending prophets (whose purpose is to convey obligation) is also impossible.

Key Terms
فائدة (benefit)
عبث (futility)
حكمة (wisdom)
لذة (pleasure)
سرور (joy)
ألم (pain)
غم (grief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why is obligation considered futile?
A) Because it harms God
B) Because God can give all benefits directly without obligation
C) Because it distracts from knowledge of God
D) Because it leads to punishment

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument claims that since God can directly provide all benefits, obligation is unnecessary and constitutes futility unbecoming of divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God could give you all joy without any obligation, would you still choose to worship Him? What does your answer reveal about your intention (niyyah)?
?
Al-Razi argues that obligation is futile because God can grant everything directly. Does this shake your understanding of why you perform acts of worship? How does it challenge you to purify your purpose?
?
Consider a time you felt burdened by religious obligations. Did you secretly think, 'This is pointless'? Now confront the argument: If God's wisdom is perfect, could obligation really be futile? Where do you personally find meaning in obligations that transcends mere utility?

The Fifth Argument: Obligating the Foreknown Unbeliever
Al-Razi presents the fifth argument concerning the futility of obligating those God knows will disbelieve.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against divine obligation: that obligating someone God knows will disbelieve leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Obligating a known future disbeliever either implies divine ignorance or leads to unjust punishment.

Al-Razi's fifth argument targets the fairness of obligation given divine foreknowledge. If God obligates a person whom He knows will reject faith, then either the obligated act occurs (implying God's knowledge was wrong) or it does not (making the person deserve punishment). Both options are unacceptable for a wise God.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth argument
Find the section beginning with 'الخامس' and read it carefully. Note the two possible outcomes and their implications.
The argument is concise but powerful.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
Al-Razi presents a dilemma: if the act occurs, God is ignorant (since He knew it wouldn't); if the act doesn't occur, the person is punished without benefit. Both are problematic.
This challenges the justice and omniscience of God if obligation exists.
3
Reflect on the concept of 'تجهيل' (making ignorant)
The term 'تجهيل' refers to attributing ignorance to God. If the obligated act comes to pass contrary to God's knowledge, it would make God ignorant, which is impossible.
4
Connect to the overall doubt
This argument builds on the previous ones by showing that obligation leads to logical or moral absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون
حال رجحان أحد الداعيين على الآخر، أما حال الاستواء فمحال؛ لأن الفعل ترجح، وحصول الرجحان حال حصول الاستواء جمع بين النقيضين، وأما حال الرجحان فمحال ؛ لأن الراجح واجب والمرجوح ممتنع - على ما بينا تقرير هذه المقدمة في مواضع - وإن وقع التكليف بالراجح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع شيء واجب الوقوع، وإن كان تكليفا بالمرجوح كان ذلك تكليفا بإيقاع ممتنع الوقوع، وتمام هذا الوجه قد تقدم في مسألة خلق الأفعال.
تقرير
الرابع : أن التكليف لا فائدة فيه البتة، فكان عبئًا غير لائق بحكمة الحكيم. وإنما قلنا إنه لا فائدة فيه؛ لأن تلك الفائدة إن عادت إلى المعبود لزم أن يكون في محل النفع والضرر والزيادة والنقصان وهو محال وإن عادت إلى العابد فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن جميع الفوائد محصورة في أمرين : إما اللذة وإما السرور، أو ما يكون مفضيًا إليهما، وإما دفع الألم أو دفع الغم، أو ما يكون مفضيا إليهما، والمعبود تعالى قادر على تحصيل كل ذلك للعبد بدون واسطة التكليف، فكان توسط التكليف عبثا، وإذا ثبت هذا فظاهر أن العبث لا يليق بأحكم الحاكمين.
الخامس: أن تكليف من علم أنه يكفر أو يفسق غير لائق بالحكمة؛ لأن المكلف به إذا دخل في الوجود لزم تجهيل المعبود، وإن لم يدخل في الوجود لزم استحقاق العبد للعقاب، وفعل شيء لا فائدة فيه - إلا أحد هذين الأمرين المحذورين - لا يليق بالحكمة.
السادس : أن التكليف يقتضي شغل القلب بتلك الأعمال، واشتغال القلب بغير يمنعه عن الاستغراق في معرفة الله ومحبته، وكل ما كان مانعا من معرفة الله ومحبته كان تركه من أوجب الواجبات.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الستة أن القول بالتكليف باطل، وإذا ثبت هذا كان القول بالبعثة أيضًا باطلا؛ لأنه لا فائدة من البعثة إلا توجيه التكاليف على الخلق، وإذا كان المقصود باطلا كان التبع أولى بالبطلان.
The Thirtieth Issue: The case of preponderance of one of the two motives over the other. As for the case of equality, it is impossible because the action would be preponderant, and obtaining preponderance while equality obtains is a conjunction of contradictories. As for the case of preponderance, it is impossible because the preponderant is necessary and the inferior is impossible—according to what we have established of this premise in other places. If obligation (taklif) falls upon the preponderant, it is an obligation to bring about something necessarily occurring; and if it falls upon the inferior, it is an obligation to bring about something impossible of occurrence. The completion of this point has preceded in the issue of the creation of acts. Summary: Fourth: Obligation has absolutely no benefit, so it is a burden unbefitting the wisdom of the Wise. We say it has no benefit because if that benefit redounds to the Worshipped, it would necessitate that He is subject to benefit and harm, increase and decrease—which is impossible. And if it redounds to the worshipper, it is also false, because all benefits are confined to two things: either pleasure and joy, or what leads to them, or the repulsion of pain or grief, or what leads to them. And the Worshipped, exalted, is able to achieve all of that for the servant without the intermediary of obligation, so the intermediation of obligation is futility. If this is established, it is clear that futility is not befitting the Most Wise of judges. Fifth: Obligating one whom He knows will disbelieve or act wickedly is unbefitting of wisdom, because if the obligated act comes into existence, it necessitates ignorance of the Worshipped; and if it does not come into existence, it necessitates the servant's deserving punishment. Doing something with no benefit except one of these two objectionable things is not befitting of wisdom. Sixth: Obligation necessitates occupying the heart with those acts, and occupying the heart with other than [God] prevents it from absorption in knowledge of God and love of Him. Anything that prevents knowledge of God and love of Him, its abandonment is among the most obligatory duties. Thus, by these six aspects it is established that the claim of obligation is false. If this is established, the claim of sending messengers is also false, because there is no benefit from sending messengers except directing obligations upon creation. If the purpose is false, the subordinate is more deserving of falsehood.

The Sixth Argument: Distraction from Knowledge and Love of God
Al-Razi presents the sixth argument that obligation distracts from knowledge and love of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument against divine obligation: that obligation occupies the heart with other than God, preventing the highest good of knowledge and love of Him.

Core Idea
Obligation distracts from the ultimate purpose of knowing and loving God.

Al-Razi's sixth and final argument in this doubt claims that religious obligations preoccupy the heart with acts instead of God. Since the highest goal is absorption in knowledge and love of God, anything that prevents this should be abandoned. Therefore, obligation becomes a hindrance rather than a help.

Guided Path
1
Read the sixth argument
Find the section starting with 'السادس' and read it. Note how al-Razi prioritizes knowledge and love of God.
This argument appeals to spiritual values.
2
Identify the core claim
Obligation, by requiring acts, occupies the heart. This occupation prevents the heart from being absorbed in knowledge and love of God.
3
Understand the normative conclusion
Since knowledge and love of God are the most obligatory duties, anything that prevents them should be abandoned. Hence, obligation itself should be abandoned.
This is a radical conclusion: obligations are counterproductive.
4
Connect to the overall doubt conclusion
This argument completes the doubt. Al-Razi then concludes that if obligation is false, prophethood (which conveys obligation) is also false.

Source Text

According to the sixth argument, what is the most obligatory duty?
A) Performing the obligation
B) Abandoning whatever prevents knowledge and love of God
C) Seeking worldly benefit
D) Avoiding punishment

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one obligatory act you perform regularly. In the next performance, observe your heart: is it truly absorbed in God, or is it occupied by the act itself? If the latter, what would it take for you to shift from duty to direct love?
?
Al-Razi suggests that anything that prevents knowledge and love of God must be abandoned, even if it is normally obligatory. Is there anything in your religious life—a habit, a practice, a routine—that you suspect might be distracting you from God rather than drawing you near? Are you willing to leave it for His sake?
?
If you were to spend one hour today without any prescribed worship, simply trying to know and love God through stillness and thought, would your heart feel empty or full? What does your discomfort reveal about your reliance on actions rather than on Him?

Prophethood

The Second Doubt: Moral Knowledge by Reason Alone
Al-Razi records the claim that all moral judgments are known by reason, making prophethood needless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that claims reason alone can judge all actions, thereby rendering prophethood unnecessary.

Core Idea
If reason knows all moral judgments, prophethood becomes futile.

Al-Razi presents the second objection from those who deny prophethood. They argue that every action's moral status is either known by reason to be good, known to be bad, or unknown. In the unknown case, reason can still determine whether to do it or not. Thus, there is no need for a divine messenger to inform us of moral duties. This passage challenges the necessity of prophethood based on the sufficiency of human reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi uses a logical division here.
2
Identify the tripartite division of actions
The passage divides actions into three categories: those whose goodness is known, those whose badness is known, and those whose moral status is unknown. Find these three in the text.
This sets up the structure of the argument.
3
Analyze the treatment of unknown actions
For unknown actions, the argument subdivides them into necessary and non-necessary. Understand how reason handles each subcategory.
Necessary actions must be done; non-necessary actions must be avoided due to potential harm.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that since all actions are covered by reason (either known or determined by reason), there is no need for a messenger. Sending one would be futile.
This directly challenges the necessity of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشبهة الثانية لمنكري النبوات أن الأفعال إما أن يكون حسنها معلوما، أو يكون قبحها معلوما ، أو لا نعلم لا حسنها ولا قبحها، فإن كان حسنها معلومًا عملناه، ولا حاجة بنا في ذلك إلى تعريف الشرع، وإن كان قبحها معلوما تركناه، وإن لم نعلم لا حسنها ولا قبحها فهذا الشيء إما أن يكون فعله اضطراريًا كالتنفس في الهواء، وما يجري مجراه وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، فإن كان فعله اضطراريًا كان جورًا لا محالة، لأن تكليف ما لا يطاق لا يليق بالحكمة، وإن لم يكن فعله اضطراريًا وجب علينا تركه ؛ لأن مثل هذا الشيء لا حاجة بنا إلى فعله، وفي فعله خطر، واحتمال ضرر، وكل ما كان كذلك اقتضى العقل تركه.
فثبت بما ذكرنا أن جملة أحكام الأفعال في الفعل والترك معلومة لنا، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن في بعثة الرسول فائدة، فتكون البعثة عبثا والعبث غير جائز على الحكيم.
The second doubt of the deniers of prophethood: Actions are either such that their goodness is known, or their badness is known, or we know neither their goodness nor their badness. If their goodness is known, we do it, and we have no need in that for definition by the Law. If their badness is known, we abandon it. If we know neither their goodness nor badness, then this thing is either such that its doing is necessary, like breathing air and the like, or it is not like that. If its doing is necessary, it is injustice undoubtedly, because obligating what cannot be borne is not befitting wisdom. If its doing is not necessary, then we must abandon it, because we have no need to do such a thing, and in doing it there is danger and possibility of harm. Everything that is such requires reason to abandon it. Thus, it is established by what we have mentioned that all the judgments of actions concerning doing and abandoning are known to us. If that is the case, there is no benefit in sending a messenger, so sending a messenger is futility, and futility is not permissible for the Wise.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents the second doubt of the deniers of prophethood. It argues that all moral judgments about actions—whether they are good, bad, or neutral—are known by reason alone, so there is no need for divine revelation. The argument divides actions into three categories: those known to be good (we do them), those known to be bad (we avoid them), and those whose moral status is unknown. For unknown actions, if they are necessary (like breathing), we must do them; if they are not necessary, reason requires avoiding them due to potential harm. Since all actions are covered by reason, sending a messenger to declare what is already known is futile.

Key Terms
حسن (goodness)
قبح (badness)
اضطراري (necessary)
تكليف ما لا يطاق (obligation of what cannot be borne)
شرع (Law)
بعثة (sending)
عبث (futility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second doubt argues that reason alone can judge all actions, thereby eliminating the need for prophethood and rendering it futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The doubt claims that prophethood is futile if reason knows everything. But when you face a real moral dilemma where your reason is confused, do you turn to the Prophet’s guidance? Or do you trust your own judgment and ignore what Allah has sent?

The Third Doubt: Irrationality of Religious Practices
Al-Razi records the objection that religious rituals are arbitrary and irrational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third doubt of the deniers of prophethood, which claims that religious practices like Hajj and the distinction between similar days are irrational and arbitrary.

Core Idea
The third doubt claims religious rituals are arbitrary, exemplified by similar days having opposite rulings.

Al-Razi presents a critical objection against prophethood: that religious laws contain actions that seem irrational. In this passage, he records the claim that rituals like Hajj and the distinction between similar days lack wisdom. Let's examine the doubt and its examples.

Guided Path

The passage gives two examples of irrationality: the rituals of Hajj (specifying a particular house for veneration) and the distinction between the last day of Ramadan and the first day of Shawwal (similar days with opposite rulings). Find these in the text.
The second example is more detailed.
3
Reflect on the claim of arbitrariness
The doubt argues that these practices are 'not befitting wisdom' because they assign special significance to things without any apparent reason. Consider why this is a challenge to prophethood.
The response to this doubt will come in later lessons.

This passage presents the third doubt of the deniers of prophethood. It claims that certain religious practices are irrational and arbitrary. Examples include specifying the Kaaba for worship, designating particular times for rituals, and distinguishing between days like the last day of Ramadan (when fasting is obligatory) and the first day of Shawwal (when fasting is prohibited) despite them being similar in all intrinsic respects. The doubt argues that such assignments without external reason are unbefitting of a wise lawgiver.
